   <p>
    <b>
     Paul-Michel Foucault
    </b>
    (
    <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap">
     <small>
      <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">
       UK
      </a>
      :
     </small>
     <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
      <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
       /
       <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
        <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
         ˈ
        </span>
        <span title="'f' in 'find'">
         f
        </span>
        <span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">
         uː
        </span>
        <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
         k
        </span>
        <span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">
         oʊ
        </span>
       </span>
       /
      </a>
     </span>
    </span>
    <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
     <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
      <span style="font-size:90%">
       FOO
      </span>
      -koh
     </i>
    </a>
    ,
    <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap">
     <small>
      <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">
       US
      </a>
      :
     </small>
     <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
      <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
       /
       <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
        <span title="'f' in 'find'">
         f
        </span>
        <span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">
         uː
        </span>
        <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
         ˈ
        </span>
        <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
         k
        </span>
        <span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">
         oʊ
        </span>
       </span>
       /
      </a>
     </span>
    </span>
    <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
     <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
      foo-
      <span style="font-size:90%">
       KOH
      </span>
     </i>
    </a>
    ;
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
     <a href="#cite_note-6">
      [6]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <small>
     French:
    </small>
    <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
     <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">
      [pɔl miʃɛl fuko]
     </a>
    </span>
    ; 15 October 1926 – 25 June 1984) was a French philosopher,
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_ideas" title="History of ideas">
     historian of ideas
    </a>
    , writer, political activist, and
    <a href="/wiki/Literary_criticism" title="Literary criticism">
     literary critic
    </a>
    .
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault's theories primarily address the relationship between
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_(philosophy)" title="Power (philosophy)">
     power
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">
     knowledge
    </a>
    , and how they are used as a form of
    <a href="/wiki/Social_control" title="Social control">
     social control
    </a>
    through societal institutions. Though often cited as a
    <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">
     structuralist
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">
     postmodernist
    </a>
    , Foucault rejected these labels.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burrell-1998_7-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Burrell-1998-7">
      [7]
     </a>
    </sup>
    His thought has influenced academics, especially those working in
    <a href="/wiki/Communication_studies" title="Communication studies">
     communication studies
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">
     anthropology
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">
     psychology
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">
     sociology
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Criminology" title="Criminology">
     criminology
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Cultural_studies" title="Cultural studies">
     cultural studies
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Literary_theory" title="Literary theory">
     literary theory
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">
     feminism
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">
     Marxism
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Critical_theory" title="Critical theory">
     critical theory
    </a>
    .
   </p>
   <p>
    Born in
    <a href="/wiki/Poitiers" title="Poitiers">
     Poitiers
    </a>
    , France, into an
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper-middle-class" title="Upper-middle-class">
     upper-middle-class
    </a>
    family, Foucault was educated at the
    <a href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e_Henri-IV" title="Lycée Henri-IV">
     Lycée Henri-IV
    </a>
    , at the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Normale_Sup%C3%A9rieure" title="École Normale Supérieure">
     École Normale Supérieure
    </a>
    , where he developed an interest in philosophy and came under the influence of his tutors
    <a href="/wiki/Jean_Hyppolite" title="Jean Hyppolite">
     Jean Hyppolite
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Louis_Althusser" title="Louis Althusser">
     Louis Althusser
    </a>
    , and at the
    <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">
     University of Paris
    </a>
    (
    <a href="/wiki/Sorbonne" title="Sorbonne">
     Sorbonne
    </a>
    ), where he earned degrees in philosophy and psychology. After several years as a cultural diplomat abroad, he returned to France and published his first major book,
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Madness_and_Civilization" title="Madness and Civilization">
      The History of Madness
     </a>
    </i>
    (1961). After obtaining work between 1960 and 1966 at the
    <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal_University" title="Blaise Pascal University">
     University of Clermont-Ferrand
    </a>
    , he produced
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_the_Clinic" title="The Birth of the Clinic">
      The Birth of the Clinic
     </a>
    </i>
    (1963) and
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_Order_of_Things" title="The Order of Things">
      The Order of Things
     </a>
    </i>
    (1966), publications which displayed his increasing involvement with
    <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">
     structuralism
    </a>
    , from which he later distanced himself. These first three histories exemplified a
    <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">
     historiographical
    </a>
    technique Foucault was developing called "archaeology."
   </p>
   <p>
    From 1966 to 1968, Foucault lectured at the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Tunis" title="University of Tunis">
     University of Tunis
    </a>
    before returning to France, where he became head of the philosophy department at the new experimental university of
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_VIII" title="Paris VIII">
     Paris VIII
    </a>
    . Foucault subsequently published
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_Archaeology_of_Knowledge" title="The Archaeology of Knowledge">
      The Archaeology of Knowledge
     </a>
    </i>
    (1969). In 1970, Foucault was admitted to the
    <a href="/wiki/Coll%C3%A8ge_de_France" title="Collège de France">
     Collège de France
    </a>
    , a membership he retained until his death. He also became active in several left-wing groups involved in campaigns against racism and
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_rights_abuses" title="Human rights abuses">
     human rights abuses
    </a>
    and for
    <a href="/wiki/Prison_reform" title="Prison reform">
     penal reform
    </a>
    . Foucault later published
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Discipline_and_Punish" title="Discipline and Punish">
      Discipline and Punish
     </a>
    </i>
    (1975) and
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">
      The History of Sexuality
     </a>
    </i>
    (1976), in which he developed archaeological and genealogical methods which emphasized the role that power plays in society.
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault died in Paris from complications of
    <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">
     HIV/AIDS
    </a>
    ; he became the first public figure in France to die from complications of the disease. His partner
    <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defert" title="Daniel Defert">
     Daniel Defert
    </a>
    founded the
    <a href="/wiki/AIDES" title="AIDES">
     AIDES
    </a>
    charity in his memory.
   </p>
   <div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation">
    <input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/>

   <h2>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">
     Early life
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>
   <h3>
    <span id="Early_years:_1926.E2.80.931938">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years:_1926–1938">
     Early years: 1926–1938
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years: 1926–1938">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Paul-Michel Foucault was born on 15 October 1926 in the city of
    <a href="/wiki/Poitiers" title="Poitiers">
     Poitiers
    </a>
    , west-central France, as the second of three children in a prosperous,
    <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">
     socially-conservative
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Upper_middle_class" title="Upper middle class">
     upper-middle-class
    </a>
    family.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey19933Miller199339_8-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey19933Miller199339-8">
      [8]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Family tradition prescribed naming him after his father, Paul Foucault (1893–1959), but his mother insisted on the addition of Michel; referred to as Paul at school, he expressed a preference for "Michel" throughout his life.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19914–5Macey19933Miller199339_9-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19914–5Macey19933Miller199339-9">
      [9]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    His father, a successful local surgeon born in
    <a href="/wiki/Fontainebleau" title="Fontainebleau">
     Fontainebleau
    </a>
    , moved to
    <a href="/wiki/Poitiers" title="Poitiers">
     Poitiers
    </a>
    , where he set up his own practice.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19931–2_10-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19931–2-10">
      [10]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He married Anne Malapert, the daughter of prosperous surgeon Dr. Prosper Malapert, who owned a private practice and taught anatomy at the University of Poitiers' School of Medicine.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19931_11-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19931-11">
      [11]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Paul Foucault eventually took over his father-in-law's medical practice, while Anne took charge of their large mid-19th-century house, Le Piroir, in the village of
    <a href="/wiki/Vendeuvre-du-Poitou" title="Vendeuvre-du-Poitou">
     Vendeuvre-du-Poitou
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19932_12-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19932-12">
      [12]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Together the couple had three children—a girl named Francine and two boys, Paul-Michel and Denys—who all shared the same fair hair and bright blue eyes.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19933_13-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19933-13">
      [13]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The children were raised to be nominal Catholics, attending mass at the Church of Saint-Porchair, and while Michel briefly became an
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altar_boy" title="Altar boy">
     altar boy
    </a>
    , none of the family was devout.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19934_14-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19915Macey19934-14">
      [14]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In later life, Foucault revealed very little about his childhood.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey19934Miller199339_15-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey19934Miller199339-15">
      [15]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Describing himself as a "juvenile delinquent," he claimed his father was a "bully" who sternly punished him.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199339_16-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199339-16">
      [16]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1930, two years early, Foucault began his schooling at the local
    <a href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e_Henri-IV" title="Lycée Henri-IV">
     Lycée Henry-IV
    </a>
    . There he undertook two years of elementary education before entering the main
    <i>
     <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e" title="Lycée">
      lycée
     </a>
    </i>
    , where he stayed until 1936. Afterwards, he took his first four years of secondary education at the same establishment, excelling in French, Greek, Latin, and history, though doing poorly at mathematics, including
    <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">
     arithmetic
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey19938–9_17-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey19938–9-17">
      [17]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span id="Teens_to_young_adulthood:_1939.E2.80.931945">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Teens_to_young_adulthood:_1939–1945">
     Teens to young adulthood: 1939–1945
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Teens to young adulthood: 1939–1945">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    In 1939, the Second World War began, followed by
    <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
     Nazi Germany
    </a>
    's occupation of France in 1940. Foucault's parents opposed the occupation and the
    <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">
     Vichy regime
    </a>
    , but did not join the
    <a href="/wiki/French_Resistance" title="French Resistance">
     Resistance
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey19937_18-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey19937-18">
      [18]
     </a>
    </sup>
    That year, Foucault's mother enrolled him in the Collège Saint-Stanislas, a strict Catholic institution run by the
    <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">
     Jesuits
    </a>
    . Although he later described his years there as an "ordeal," Foucault excelled academically, particularly in philosophy, history, and literature.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19916–7Macey199310Miller199339–40Smart200219_19-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19916–7Macey199310Miller199339–40Smart200219-19">
      [19]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1942 he entered his final year, the
    <i>
     terminale
    </i>
    , where he focused on the study of philosophy, earning his
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Baccalaur%C3%A9at" title="Baccalauréat">
      baccalauréat
     </a>
    </i>
    in 1943.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199310_20-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199310-20">
      [20]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Returning to the local Lycée Henry-IV, he studied history and philosophy for a year,
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199313_21-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199313-21">
      [21]
     </a>
    </sup>
    aided by a personal tutor, the philosopher
    <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Louis_Girard_(philosopher)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Louis Girard (philosopher) (page does not exist)">
     Louis Girard
    </a>
    <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
     [
     <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Girard" title="fr:Louis Girard">
      fr
     </a>
     ]
    </span>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19919Macey199311_22-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19919Macey199311-22">
      [22]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Rejecting his father's wishes that he become a surgeon, in 1945 Foucault went to Paris, where he enrolled in one of the country's most prestigious secondary schools, which was also known as the
    <a href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e_Henri-IV" title="Lycée Henri-IV">
     Lycée Henri-IV
    </a>
    . Here he studied under the philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Jean_Hyppolite" title="Jean Hyppolite">
     Jean Hyppolite
    </a>
    , an
    <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">
     existentialist
    </a>
    and expert on the work of 19th-century German philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">
     Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel
    </a>
    . Hyppolite had devoted himself to uniting existentialist theories with the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialectics" title="Dialectics">
     dialectical theories
    </a>
    of Hegel and
    <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">
     Karl Marx
    </a>
    . These ideas influenced Foucault, who adopted Hyppolite's conviction that philosophy must develop through a study of history.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199111,_14–21Macey199315–17Miller199340–41_23-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199111,_14–21Macey199315–17Miller199340–41-23">
      [23]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span id="University_studies:_1946.E2.80.931951">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="University_studies:_1946–1951">
     University studies: 1946–1951
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: University studies: 1946–1951">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">
    .mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}
   </style>
   <blockquote class="templatequote" style="float:right; width:33%;">
    <p>
     I wasn't always smart, I was actually very stupid in school ... [T]here was a boy who was very attractive who was even stupider than I was. And to ingratiate myself with this boy who was very beautiful, I began to do his homework for him—and that's how I became smart, I had to do all this work to just keep ahead of him a little bit, to help him. In a sense, all the rest of my life I've been trying to do intellectual things that would attract beautiful boys.
    </p>
    <div class="templatequotecite">
     —
     <cite>
      Michel Foucault, 1983
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199356_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199356-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </cite>
    </div>
   </blockquote>
   <p>
    In autumn 1946, attaining excellent results, Foucault was admitted to the élite
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Normale_Sup%C3%A9rieure" title="École Normale Supérieure">
     École Normale Supérieure
    </a>
    (ENS), for which he undertook exams and an oral interrogation by
    <a href="/wiki/Georges_Canguilhem" title="Georges Canguilhem">
     Georges Canguilhem
    </a>
    and Pierre-Maxime Schuhl to gain entry. Of the hundred students entering the ENS, Foucault ranked fourth based on his entry results, and encountered the highly competitive nature of the institution. Like most of his classmates, he lived in the school's communal dormitories on the Parisian Rue d'Ulm.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199124–25Macey199317–22Miller199345_25-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199124–25Macey199317–22Miller199345-25">
      [25]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    He remained largely unpopular, spending much time alone, reading voraciously.  His fellow students noted his love of violence and the macabre; he decorated his bedroom with images of torture and war drawn during the
    <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">
     Napoleonic Wars
    </a>
    by Spanish artist
    <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Goya" title="Francisco Goya">
     Francisco Goya
    </a>
    , and on one occasion chased a classmate with a dagger.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199126Miller199345_26-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199126Miller199345-26">
      [26]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Prone to
    <a href="/wiki/Self-harm" title="Self-harm">
     self-harm
    </a>
    , in 1948 Foucault allegedly
    <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attempt" title="Suicide attempt">
     attempted suicide
    </a>
    ; his father sent him to see the psychiatrist
    <a href="/wiki/Jean_Delay" title="Jean Delay">
     Jean Delay
    </a>
    at the
    <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Anne_Hospital_Center" title="Sainte-Anne Hospital Center">
     Sainte-Anne Hospital Center
    </a>
    .  Obsessed with the idea of self-mutilation and suicide, Foucault attempted the latter several times in ensuing years, praising suicide in later writings.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199126Macey199327–28Miller199354–55_27-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199126Macey199327–28Miller199354–55-27">
      [27]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The ENS's doctor examined Foucault's state of mind, suggesting that his suicidal tendencies emerged from the distress surrounding his homosexuality, because same-sex sexual activity was socially taboo in France.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199126Miller1993_28-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199126Miller1993-28">
      [28]
     </a>
    </sup>
    At the time, Foucault engaged in homosexual activity with men whom he encountered in the underground Parisian
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gay_scene" title="Gay scene">
     gay scene
    </a>
    , also indulging in drug use; according to biographer
    <a href="/wiki/James_Miller_(academic)" title="James Miller (academic)">
     James Miller
    </a>
    , he enjoyed the thrill and sense of danger that these activities offered him.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199330Miller199355–56_29-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199330Miller199355–56-29">
      [29]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Although studying various subjects, Foucault soon gravitated towards philosophy, reading not only Hegel and Marx but also
    <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">
     Immanuel Kant
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Husserl" title="Edmund Husserl">
     Edmund Husserl
    </a>
    and most significantly,
    <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">
     Martin Heidegger
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199334Miller199346_30-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199334Miller199346-30">
      [30]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He began reading the publications of philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Bachelard" title="Gaston Bachelard">
     Gaston Bachelard
    </a>
    , taking a particular interest in his work exploring the
    <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">
     history of science
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199335Miller199360–61_31-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199335Miller199360–61-31">
      [31]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He graduated from the ENS with a
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Licence_(France)" title="Licence (France)">
     B.A. (licence)
    </a>
    in Philosophy in 1948
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schrift_p._126_1-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Schrift_p._126-1">
      [1]
     </a>
    </sup>
    and a DES (
    <i>
     <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dipl%C3%B4me_d%27%C3%A9tudes_sup%C3%A9rieures&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Diplôme d'études supérieures (page does not exist)">
      diplôme d'études supérieures
     </a>
     <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
      [
      <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/dipl%C3%B4me_d%27%C3%A9tudes_sup%C3%A9rieures" title="fr:diplôme d'études supérieures">
       fr
      </a>
      ]
     </span>
    </i>
    , roughly equivalent to an
    <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts" title="Master of Arts">
     M.A.
    </a>
    ) in Philosophy in 1949.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schrift_p._126_1-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-Schrift_p._126-1">
      [1]
     </a>
    </sup>
    His DES thesis under the direction of Hyppolite was titled
    <i>
     La Constitution d'un transcendental dans La Phénoménologie de l'esprit de Hegel
    </i>
    (
    <i>
     The Constitution of a Historical Transcendental in Hegel's
     <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomenology_of_Spirit" title="Phenomenology of Spirit">
      Phenomenology of Spirit
     </a>
    </i>
    ).
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schrift_p._126_1-3">
     <a href="#cite_note-Schrift_p._126-1">
      [1]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In 1948, the philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Louis_Althusser" title="Louis Althusser">
     Louis Althusser
    </a>
    became a tutor at the ENS. A
    <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">
     Marxist
    </a>
    , he influenced both Foucault and a number of other students, encouraging them to join the
    <a href="/wiki/French_Communist_Party" title="French Communist Party">
     French Communist Party
    </a>
    (
    <i>
     Parti communiste français
    </i>
    , PCF). Foucault did so in 1950, but never became particularly active in its activities, and never adopted an
    <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Marxism" title="Orthodox Marxism">
     orthodox Marxist
    </a>
    viewpoint, rejecting core Marxist tenets such as
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_struggle" title="Class struggle">
     class struggle
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199132–36,_51–55Macey199323–26,_37–40Miller199357_32-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199132–36,_51–55Macey199323–26,_37–40Miller199357-32">
      [32]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He soon became dissatisfied with the bigotry that he experienced within the party's ranks; he personally faced
    <a href="/wiki/Homophobia" title="Homophobia">
     homophobia
    </a>
    and was appalled by the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-semitism" title="Anti-semitism">
     anti-semitism
    </a>
    exhibited during the 1952–53 "
    <a href="/wiki/Doctors%27_plot" title="Doctors' plot">
     Doctors' plot
    </a>
    " in the
    <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
     Soviet Union
    </a>
    . He left the Communist Party in 1953, but remained Althusser's friend and defender for the rest of his life.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199156–57Macey199339–40Miller199357–58_33-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199156–57Macey199339–40Miller199357–58-33">
      [33]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Although failing at the first attempt in 1950, he passed his
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Agr%C3%A9gation" title="Agrégation">
      agrégation
     </a>
    </i>
    in philosophy on the second try, in 1951.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199136–38Macey199343–45Miller199361_34-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199136–38Macey199343–45Miller199361-34">
      [34]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Excused from
    <a href="/wiki/National_service" title="National service">
     national service
    </a>
    on medical grounds, he decided to start a doctorate at the
    <a href="/wiki/Fondation_Dosne-Thiers" title="Fondation Dosne-Thiers">
     Fondation Thiers
    </a>
    in 1951, focusing on the philosophy of psychology,
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199139–40Macey199345–46,_49_35-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199139–40Macey199345–46,_49-35">
      [35]
     </a>
    </sup>
    but he relinquished it after only one year in 1952.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
     <a href="#cite_note-36">
      [36]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault was also interested in psychology and he attended
    <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Lagache" title="Daniel Lagache">
     Daniel Lagache
    </a>
    's lectures at the University of Paris, where he obtained a
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Licence_(France)" title="Licence (France)">
     B.A. (licence)
    </a>
    in Psychology in 1949 and a
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specialist_diploma" title="Specialist diploma">
     Diploma
    </a>
    in
    <a href="/wiki/Psychopathology" title="Psychopathology">
     Psychopathology
    </a>
    (
    <i>
     Diplôme de psychopathologie
    </i>
    ) from the University's Institute of Psychology (now
    <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Institut_de_psychologie_de_l%27universit%C3%A9_Paris_Descartes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Institut de psychologie de l'université Paris Descartes (page does not exist)">
     Institut de psychologie de l'université Paris Descartes
    </a>
    <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
     [
     <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institut_de_psychologie_de_l%27universit%C3%A9_Paris_Descartes" title="fr:Institut de psychologie de l'université Paris Descartes">
      fr
     </a>
     ]
    </span>
    ) in June 1952.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schrift_p._126_1-4">
     <a href="#cite_note-Schrift_p._126-1">
      [1]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h2>
    <span id="Early_career_.281951.E2.80.931960.29">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career_(1951–1960)">
     Early career (1951–1960)
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early career (1951–1960)">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>
   <div class="thumb tright">
    <div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;">
     <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche187a.jpg">
      <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1986" data-file-width="1464" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nietzsche187a.jpg/175px-Nietzsche187a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nietzsche187a.jpg/263px-Nietzsche187a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nietzsche187a.jpg/350px-Nietzsche187a.jpg 2x" width="175"/>
     </a>
     <div class="thumbcaption">
      <div class="magnify">
       <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nietzsche187a.jpg" title="Enlarge">
       </a>
      </div>
      In the early 1950s, Foucault came under the influence of German philosopher Friedrich Nietzsche, who remained a core influence on his work throughout his life.
     </div>
    </div>
   </div>
   <p>
    Over the following few years, Foucault embarked on a variety of research and teaching jobs.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199361_37-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199361-37">
      [37]
     </a>
    </sup>
    From 1951 to 1955, he worked as a psychology instructor at the ENS at Althusser's invitation.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199150Macey199349Miller199362_38-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199150Macey199349Miller199362-38">
      [38]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In Paris, he shared a flat with his brother, who was training to become a surgeon, but for three days in the week commuted to the northern town of
    <a href="/wiki/Lille" title="Lille">
     Lille
    </a>
    , teaching psychology at the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universit%C3%A9_Lille_Nord_de_France" title="Université Lille Nord de France">
     Université de Lille
    </a>
    from 1953 to 1954.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199161–62Macey199347_39-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199161–62Macey199347-39">
      [39]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Many of his students liked his lecturing style.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199356_40-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199356-40">
      [40]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Meanwhile, he continued working on his thesis, visiting the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_Nationale" title="Bibliothèque Nationale">
     Bibliothèque Nationale
    </a>
    every day to read the work of psychologists like
    <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Pavlov" title="Ivan Pavlov">
     Ivan Pavlov
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Jean_Piaget" title="Jean Piaget">
     Jean Piaget
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Karl_Jaspers" title="Karl Jaspers">
     Karl Jaspers
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199349Miller199361–62_41-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199349Miller199361–62-41">
      [41]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Undertaking research at the psychiatric institute of the Sainte-Anne Hospital, he became an unofficial intern, studying the relationship between doctor and patient and aiding experiments in the
    <a href="/wiki/Electroencephalography" title="Electroencephalography">
     electroencephalographic
    </a>
    laboratory.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199141–49Macey199356–58Miller199362_42-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199141–49Macey199356–58Miller199362-42">
      [42]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault adopted many of the theories of the psychoanalyst
    <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">
     Sigmund Freud
    </a>
    , undertaking psychoanalytical interpretation of his dreams and making friends undergo
    <a href="/wiki/Rorschach_test" title="Rorschach test">
     Rorschach tests
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199130,_43Miller199362–63_43-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199130,_43Miller199362–63-43">
      [43]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Embracing the Parisian
    <i>
     avant-garde
    </i>
    , Foucault entered into a romantic relationship with the
    <a href="/wiki/Serialism" title="Serialism">
     serialist
    </a>
    composer
    <a href="/wiki/Jean_Barraqu%C3%A9" title="Jean Barraqué">
     Jean Barraqué
    </a>
    . Together, they tried to produce their greatest work, heavily used recreational drugs and engaged in
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sado-masochism" title="Sado-masochism">
     sado-masochistic
    </a>
    sexual activity.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199165–68Macey199350–53Miller199366,_79–82,_89–91_44-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199165–68Macey199350–53Miller199366,_79–82,_89–91-44">
      [44]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In August 1953, Foucault and Barraqué holidayed in Italy, where the philosopher immersed himself in
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Untimely_Meditations" title="Untimely Meditations">
      Untimely Meditations
     </a>
    </i>
    (1873–76), a set of four essays by the philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">
     Friedrich Nietzsche
    </a>
    . Later describing Nietzsche's work as "a revelation", he felt that reading the book deeply affected him, being a watershed moment in his life.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199152Macey199350Miller199364–67_45-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199152Macey199350Miller199364–67-45">
      [45]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault subsequently experienced another groundbreaking self-revelation when watching a Parisian performance of
    <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Beckett" title="Samuel Beckett">
     Samuel Beckett
    </a>
    's new play,
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Waiting_for_Godot" title="Waiting for Godot">
      Waiting for Godot
     </a>
    </i>
    , in 1953.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199341Miller199364–65_46-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199341Miller199364–65-46">
      [46]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Interested in literature, Foucault was an avid reader of the philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Blanchot" title="Maurice Blanchot">
     Maurice Blanchot
    </a>
    's book reviews published in
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Nouvelle_Revue_Fran%C3%A7aise" title="Nouvelle Revue Française">
      Nouvelle Revue Française
     </a>
    </i>
    . Enamoured of Blanchot's literary style and critical theories, in later works he adopted Blanchot's technique of "interviewing" himself.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199158Macey199355Miller199382–84_47-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199158Macey199355Miller199382–84-47">
      [47]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault also came across
    <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Broch" title="Hermann Broch">
     Hermann Broch
    </a>
    's 1945 novel
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_Virgil" title="The Death of Virgil">
      The Death of Virgil
     </a>
    </i>
    , a work that obsessed both him and Barraqué. While the latter attempted to convert the work into an
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epic_opera" title="Epic opera">
     epic opera
    </a>
    , Foucault admired Broch's text for its portrayal of death as an affirmation of life.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199166Macey199353Miller199384–85_48-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199166Macey199353Miller199384–85-48">
      [48]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The couple took a mutual interest in the work of such authors as the
    <a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Sade" title="Marquis de Sade">
     Marquis de Sade
    </a>
    ,
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">
     Fyodor Dostoyevsky
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">
     Franz Kafka
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Jean_Genet" title="Jean Genet">
     Jean Genet
    </a>
    , all of whose works explored the themes of sex and violence.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199131Macey199351–52_49-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199131Macey199351–52-49">
      [49]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
   <blockquote class="templatequote" style="float:right; width:33%;">
    <p>
     I belong to that generation who, as students, had before their eyes, and were limited by, a horizon consisting of Marxism, phenomenology and existentialism. For me the break was first Beckett's
     <i>
      Waiting for Godot
     </i>
     , a breathtaking performance.
    </p>
    <div class="templatequotecite">
     —
     <cite>
      Michel Foucault, 1983
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199365_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199365-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </cite>
    </div>
   </blockquote>
   <p>
    Interested in the work of Swiss psychologist
    <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Binswanger" title="Ludwig Binswanger">
     Ludwig Binswanger
    </a>
    , Foucault aided family friend Jacqueline Verdeaux in translating his works into French. Foucault was particularly interested in Binswanger's studies of
    <a href="/wiki/Ellen_West" title="Ellen West">
     Ellen West
    </a>
    who, like himself, had a deep obsession with suicide, eventually killing herself.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199144–45Macey199359–61Miller199373–75_51-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199144–45Macey199359–61Miller199373–75-51">
      [51]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1954, Foucault authored an introduction to Binswanger's paper "Dream and Existence", in which he argued that dreams constituted "the birth of the world" or "the heart laid bare," expressing the mind's deepest desires.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199145–46Macey199367–69Miller199376–77_52-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199145–46Macey199367–69Miller199376–77-52">
      [52]
     </a>
    </sup>
    That same year, Foucault published his first book,
    <i>
     Maladie mentale et personalité
    </i>
    ("Mental Illness and Personality"), in which he exhibited his influence from both Marxist and Heideggerian thought, covering a wide range of subject matter from the reflex psychology of Pavlov to the classic psychoanalysis of Freud. Referencing the work of
    <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">
     sociologists
    </a>
    and anthropologists such as
    <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">
     Émile Durkheim
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Mead" title="Margaret Mead">
     Margaret Mead
    </a>
    , he presented his theory that illness was culturally relative.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199168–70Macey199363–66Miller199363_53-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199168–70Macey199363–66Miller199363-53">
      [53]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Biographer
    <a href="/wiki/James_Miller_(academic)" title="James Miller (academic)">
     James Miller
    </a>
    noted that while the book exhibited "erudition and evident intelligence," it lacked the "kind of fire and flair" which Foucault exhibited in subsequent works.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199363_54-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199363-54">
      [54]
     </a>
    </sup>
    It was largely critically ignored, receiving only one review at the time.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199367_55-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199367-55">
      [55]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault grew to despise it, unsuccessfully attempting to prevent its republication and translation into English.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199170_56-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199170-56">
      [56]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span id="Sweden.2C_Poland.2C_and_West_Germany:_1955.E2.80.931960">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden,_Poland,_and_West_Germany:_1955–1960">
     Sweden, Poland, and West Germany: 1955–1960
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sweden, Poland, and West Germany: 1955–1960">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Foucault spent the next five years abroad, first in Sweden, working as cultural diplomat at the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Uppsala" title="University of Uppsala">
     University of Uppsala
    </a>
    , a job obtained through his acquaintance with historian of religion
    <a href="/wiki/Georges_Dum%C3%A9zil" title="Georges Dumézil">
     Georges Dumézil
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199173–74Macey199370–71_57-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199173–74Macey199370–71-57">
      [57]
     </a>
    </sup>
    At
    <a href="/wiki/Uppsala" title="Uppsala">
     Uppsala
    </a>
    he was appointed a Reader in French language and literature, while simultaneously working as director of the Maison de France, thus opening the possibility of a cultural-diplomatic career.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199176–78Macey199373,_76_58-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199176–78Macey199373,_76-58">
      [58]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Although finding it difficult to adjust to the "Nordic gloom" and long winters, he developed close friendships with two Frenchmen, biochemist Jean-François Miquel and physicist Jacques Papet-Lépine, and entered into romantic and sexual relationships with various men. In Uppsala he became known for his heavy alcohol consumption and reckless driving in his new
    <a href="/wiki/Jaguar_Cars" title="Jaguar Cars">
     Jaguar car
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199174–77Macey199374–75_59-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199174–77Macey199374–75-59">
      [59]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In spring 1956 Barraqué broke from his relationship with Foucault, announcing that he wanted to leave the "vertigo of madness".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199168Macey199381Miller199391_60-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199168Macey199381Miller199391-60">
      [60]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In Uppsala, Foucault spent much of his spare time in the university's
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Carolina_Rediviva" title="Carolina Rediviva">
      Carolina Rediviva
     </a>
    </i>
    library, making use of their Bibliotheca Walleriana collection of texts on the history of medicine for his ongoing research.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199378_61-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199378-61">
      [61]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Finishing his doctoral thesis, Foucault hoped that Uppsala University would accept it, but
    <a href="/wiki/Sten_Lindroth" title="Sten Lindroth">
     Sten Lindroth
    </a>
    , a
    <a href="/wiki/Positivism" title="Positivism">
     positivistic
    </a>
    historian of science there, remained unimpressed, asserting that it was full of speculative generalisations and was a poor work of history; he refused to allow Foucault to be awarded a doctorate at Uppsala. In part because of this rejection, Foucault left Sweden.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199183–86Macey199379–80_62-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199183–86Macey199379–80-62">
      [62]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Later, Foucault admitted that the work was a first draft with certain lack of quality.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991190_63-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991190-63">
      [63]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Again at Dumézil's behest, in October 1958 Foucault arrived in the capital of
    <a href="/wiki/Polish_People%27s_Republic" title="Polish People's Republic">
     Polish People's Republic
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">
     Warsaw
    </a>
    and took charge of the
    <a href="/wiki/University_of_Warsaw" title="University of Warsaw">
     University of Warsaw
    </a>
    's Centre Français.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199187Macey199384Miller199391_64-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199187Macey199384Miller199391-64">
      [64]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault found life in Poland difficult due to the lack of material goods and services following the destruction of the Second World War. Witnessing the aftermath of the
    <a href="/wiki/Polish_October" title="Polish October">
     Polish October
    </a>
    of 1956, when students had protested against the governing communist
    <a href="/wiki/Polish_United_Workers%27_Party" title="Polish United Workers' Party">
     Polish United Workers' Party
    </a>
    , he felt that most Poles despised their government as a
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puppet_regime" title="Puppet regime">
     puppet regime
    </a>
    of the
    <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
     Soviet Union
    </a>
    , and thought that the system ran "badly".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991194Macey199384–85_65-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991194Macey199384–85-65">
      [65]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Considering the university a liberal enclave, he traveled the country giving lectures; proving popular, he adopted the position of
    <i>
     de facto
    </i>
    cultural attaché.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199188Macey199385–86_66-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199188Macey199385–86-66">
      [66]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Like France and Sweden, Poland legally tolerated but socially frowned on homosexual activity, and Foucault undertook relationships with a number of men; one was with a Polish security agent who hoped to trap Foucault in an embarrassing situation, which therefore would reflect badly on the French embassy. Wracked in diplomatic scandal, he was ordered
    <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
     [
     <i>
      <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
       <span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (August 2020)">
        by whom?
       </span>
      </a>
     </i>
     ]
    </sup>
    to leave Poland for a new destination.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199189Macey199386–87_67-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199189Macey199386–87-67">
      [67]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Various positions were available in
    <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">
     West Germany
    </a>
    , and so Foucault relocated to the
    <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Institut_fran%C3%A7ais_Hamburg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Institut français Hamburg (page does not exist)">
     Institut français Hamburg
    </a>
    <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
     [
     <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institut_fran%C3%A7ais_Hamburg" title="de:Institut français Hamburg">
      de
     </a>
     ]
    </span>
    (where he served as director in 1958–60), teaching the same courses he had given in Uppsala and Warsaw.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199189–90Macey199387–88_68-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199189–90Macey199387–88-68">
      [68]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21st_Century_69-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-21st_Century-69">
      [69]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Spending much time in the
    <a href="/wiki/Reeperbahn" title="Reeperbahn">
     Reeperbahn
    </a>
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_light_district" title="Red light district">
     red light district
    </a>
    , he entered into a relationship with a
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transvestite" title="Transvestite">
     transvestite
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199388_70-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199388-70">
      [70]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h2>
    <span id="Growing_career_.281960.E2.80.931970.29">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Growing_career_(1960–1970)">
     Growing career (1960–1970)
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Growing career (1960–1970)">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Madness_and_Civilization:_1960">
     <i>
      Madness and Civilization
     </i>
     : 1960
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Madness and Civilization: 1960">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
   <blockquote class="templatequote" style="float:right; width:33%;">
    <p>
     Histoire de la folie
     <i>
      is not an easy text to read, and it defies attempts to summarise its contents. Foucault refers to a bewildering variety of sources, ranging from well-known authors such as
      <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">
       Erasmus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Molière">
       Molière
      </a>
      to archival documents and forgotten figures in the history of medicine and psychiatry. His erudition derives from years pondering, to cite
      <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">
       Poe
      </a>
      , '
      <a href="/wiki/The_Raven" title="The Raven">
       over many a quaint and curious volume of forgotten lore
      </a>
      ', and his learning is not always worn lightly.
     </i>
    </p>
    <div class="templatequotecite">
     —
     <cite>
      Foucault biographer
      <a href="/wiki/David_Macey" title="David Macey">
       David Macey
      </a>
      , 1993
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199396_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199396-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </cite>
    </div>
   </blockquote>
   <p>
    In West Germany, Foucault completed in 1960 his primary thesis (
    <i>
     thèse principale
    </i>
    ) for his
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_doctorate" title="State doctorate">
     State doctorate
    </a>
    , titled
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Madness_and_Civilization" title="Madness and Civilization">
      Folie et déraison: Histoire de la folie à l'âge classique
     </a>
    </i>
    (trans. "Madness and Insanity: History of Madness in the Classical Age"), a philosophical work based upon his studies into the
    <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine" title="History of medicine">
     history of medicine
    </a>
    . The book discussed how West European society had dealt with
    <a href="/wiki/Insanity" title="Insanity">
     madness
    </a>
    , arguing that it was a social construct distinct from
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">
     mental illness
    </a>
    . Foucault traces the evolution of the concept of madness through three phases: the
    <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
     Renaissance
    </a>
    , the later 17th and 18th centuries, and the modern experience. The work alludes to the work of French poet and playwright
    <a href="/wiki/Antonin_Artaud" title="Antonin Artaud">
     Antonin Artaud
    </a>
    , who exerted a strong influence over Foucault's thought at the time.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey1993102Miller199396_72-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey1993102Miller199396-72">
      [72]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    <i>
     Histoire de la folie
    </i>
    was an expansive work, consisting of 943 pages of text, followed by appendices and a bibliography.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991101_73-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991101-73">
      [73]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault submitted it at the
    <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">
     University of Paris
    </a>
    , although the university's regulations for awarding a State doctorate required the submission of both his main thesis and a shorter complementary thesis.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199190–92Macey199388–89_74-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199190–92Macey199388–89-74">
      [74]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Obtaining a doctorate in France at the period was a multi-step process. The first step was to obtain a
    <i>
     rapporteur
    </i>
    , or "sponsor" for the work: Foucault chose
    <a href="/wiki/Georges_Canguilhem" title="Georges Canguilhem">
     Georges Canguilhem
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991101–02Macey1993103–06_75-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991101–02Macey1993103–06-75">
      [75]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The second was to find a publisher, and as a result
    <i>
     Folie et déraison
    </i>
    was published in French in May 1961 by the company
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plon" title="Plon">
     Plon
    </a>
    , whom Foucault chose over
    <a href="/wiki/Presses_Universitaires_de_France" title="Presses Universitaires de France">
     Presses Universitaires de France
    </a>
    after being rejected by
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallimard" title="Gallimard">
     Gallimard
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991105–07Macey1993106–09Miller1993117–18_76-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991105–07Macey1993106–09Miller1993117–18-76">
      [76]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1964, a heavily abridged version was published as a mass market paperback, then translated into English for publication the following year as
    <i>
     Madness and Civilization: A History of Insanity in the Age of Reason
    </i>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991122Miller1993118_77-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991122Miller1993118-77">
      [77]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    <i>
     Folie et déraison
    </i>
    received a mixed reception in France and in foreign journals focusing on French affairs. Although it was critically acclaimed by
    <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Blanchot" title="Maurice Blanchot">
     Maurice Blanchot
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Michel_Serres" title="Michel Serres">
     Michel Serres
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Roland_Barthes" title="Roland Barthes">
     Roland Barthes
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Bachelard" title="Gaston Bachelard">
     Gaston Bachelard
    </a>
    , and
    <a href="/wiki/Fernand_Braudel" title="Fernand Braudel">
     Fernand Braudel
    </a>
    , it was largely ignored by the leftist press, much to Foucault's disappointment.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991116Macey1993113–19Miller1993118_78-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991116Macey1993113–19Miller1993118-78">
      [78]
     </a>
    </sup>
    It was notably criticised for advocating
    <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">
     metaphysics
    </a>
    by young philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Derrida" title="Jacques Derrida">
     Jacques Derrida
    </a>
    in a March 1963 lecture at the
    <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">
     University of Paris
    </a>
    . Responding with a vicious retort, Foucault criticised Derrida's interpretation of
    <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">
     René Descartes
    </a>
    . The two remained bitter rivals until reconciling in 1981.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991119–21Macey1993142–45Miller1993118–19_79-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991119–21Macey1993142–45Miller1993118–19-79">
      [79]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In the English-speaking world, the work became a significant influence on the
    <a href="/wiki/Anti-psychiatry" title="Anti-psychiatry">
     anti-psychiatry
    </a>
    movement during the 1960s; Foucault took a mixed approach to this, associating with a number of anti-psychiatrists but arguing that most of them misunderstood his work.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991122–26_80-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991122–26-80">
      [80]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault's secondary thesis (French:
    <i lang="fr">
     thèse complémentaire
    </i>
    ), written in Hamburg between 1959 and 1960, was a translation and commentary on German philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">
     Immanuel Kant
    </a>
    's
    <i>
     Anthropology from a Pragmatic Point of View
    </i>
    (1798);
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21st_Century_69-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-21st_Century-69">
      [69]
     </a>
    </sup>
    the thesis was titled "
    <a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_Kant%27s_Anthropology" title="Introduction to Kant's Anthropology">
     Introduction à l
     <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
      '
     </span>
     <i>
      Anthropologie
     </i>
    </a>
    ."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
     <a href="#cite_note-81">
      [81]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Largely consisting of Foucault's discussion of textual dating—an "archaeology of the Kantian text"—he rounded off the thesis with an evocation of Nietzsche, his biggest philosophical influence.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991110Macey199389_82-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991110Macey199389-82">
      [82]
     </a>
    </sup>
    This work's
    <i>
     rapporteur
    </i>
    was Foucault's old tutor and then-director of the ENS, Hyppolite, who was well acquainted with German philosophy.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991101_73-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991101-73">
      [73]
     </a>
    </sup>
    After both theses were championed and reviewed, he underwent his public defense of his
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctoral_thesis" title="Doctoral thesis">
     doctoral thesis
    </a>
    (
    <i>
     soutenance de thèse
    </i>
    ) on 20 May 1961.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991111–13_83-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991111–13-83">
      [83]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The academics responsible for reviewing his work were concerned about the unconventional nature of his major thesis; reviewer
    <a href="/wiki/Henri_Gouhier" title="Henri Gouhier">
     Henri Gouhier
    </a>
    noted that it was not a conventional work of history, making sweeping generalisations without sufficient particular argument, and that Foucault clearly "thinks in allegories".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1993104–05_84-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1993104–05-84">
      [84]
     </a>
    </sup>
    They all agreed however that the overall project was of merit, awarding Foucault his doctorate "despite reservations."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991115_85-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991115-85">
      [85]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span id="University_of_Clermont-Ferrand.2C_The_Birth_of_the_Clinic.2C_and_The_Order_of_Things:_1960.E2.80.931966">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="University_of_Clermont-Ferrand,_The_Birth_of_the_Clinic,_and_The_Order_of_Things:_1960–1966">
     University of Clermont-Ferrand,
     <i>
      The Birth of the Clinic
     </i>
     , and
     <i>
      The Order of Things
     </i>
     : 1960–1966
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: University of Clermont-Ferrand, The Birth of the Clinic, and The Order of Things: 1960–1966">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    In October 1960, Foucault took a tenured post in philosophy at the
    <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal_University" title="Blaise Pascal University">
     University of Clermont-Ferrand
    </a>
    , commuting to the city every week from Paris,
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991129Macey1993109_86-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991129Macey1993109-86">
      [86]
     </a>
    </sup>
    where he lived in a high-rise block on the rue du Dr Finlay.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey199392_87-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey199392-87">
      [87]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Responsible for teaching psychology, which was subsumed within the philosophy department, he was considered a "fascinating" but "rather traditional" teacher at Clermont.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991140–41_88-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991140–41-88">
      [88]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The department was run by
    <a href="/wiki/Jules_Vuillemin" title="Jules Vuillemin">
     Jules Vuillemin
    </a>
    , who soon developed a friendship with Foucault.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991131Macey1993109_89-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991131Macey1993109-89">
      [89]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault then took Vuillemin's job when the latter was elected to the
    <a href="/wiki/Coll%C3%A8ge_de_France" title="Collège de France">
     Collège de France
    </a>
    in 1962.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991137_90-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991137-90">
      [90]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In this position, Foucault took a dislike to another staff member whom he considered stupid:
    <a href="/wiki/Roger_Garaudy" title="Roger Garaudy">
     Roger Garaudy
    </a>
    , a senior figure in the Communist Party. Foucault made life at the university difficult for Garaudy, leading the latter to transfer to Poitiers.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991136–38Macey1993109–10_91-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991136–38Macey1993109–10-91">
      [91]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault also caused controversy by securing a university job for his lover, the philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defert" title="Daniel Defert">
     Daniel Defert
    </a>
    , with whom he retained a non-monogamous relationship for the rest of his life.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991141–42Macey199392–93,_110Halperin1997214_92-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991141–42Macey199392–93,_110Halperin1997214-92">
      [92]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <div class="thumb tleft">
    <div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;">
     <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raymond_roussel.jpg">
      <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="529" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Raymond_roussel.jpg/175px-Raymond_roussel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Raymond_roussel.jpg/263px-Raymond_roussel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Raymond_roussel.jpg/350px-Raymond_roussel.jpg 2x" width="175"/>
     </a>
     <div class="thumbcaption">
      <div class="magnify">
       <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raymond_roussel.jpg" title="Enlarge">
       </a>
      </div>
      Foucault adored the work of Raymond Roussel and wrote a literary study of it.
     </div>
    </div>
   </div>
   <p>
    Foucault maintained a keen interest in literature, publishing reviews in literary journals, including
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Tel_Quel" title="Tel Quel">
      Tel Quel
     </a>
    </i>
    and
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Nouvelle_Revue_Fran%C3%A7aise" title="Nouvelle Revue Française">
      Nouvelle Revue Française
     </a>
    </i>
    , and sitting on the editorial board of
    <i>
     Critique
    </i>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991141,_151Macey1993120–21_93-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991141,_151Macey1993120–21-93">
      [93]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In May 1963, he published a book devoted to poet, novelist, and playwright
    <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Roussel" title="Raymond Roussel">
     Raymond Roussel
    </a>
    . It was written in under two months, published by
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallimard" title="Gallimard">
     Gallimard
    </a>
    , and was described by biographer
    <a href="/wiki/David_Macey" title="David Macey">
     David Macey
    </a>
    as "a very personal book" that resulted from a "love affair" with Roussel's work. It was published in English in 1983 as
    <i>
     Death and the Labyrinth: The World of Raymond Roussel
    </i>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991145–48Macey1993124–29_94-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991145–48Macey1993124–29-94">
      [94]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Receiving few reviews, it was largely ignored.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey1993140–42_95-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey1993140–42-95">
      [95]
     </a>
    </sup>
    That same year he published a sequel to
    <i>
     Folie et déraison
    </i>
    , titled
    <i>
     Naissance de la Clinique
    </i>
    , subsequently translated as
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_Birth_of_the_Clinic" title="The Birth of the Clinic">
      The Birth of the Clinic
     </a>
     : An Archaeology of Medical Perception
    </i>
    . Shorter than its predecessor, it focused on the changes that the medical establishment underwent in the late 18th and early 19th centuries.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991152–54Macey1993130–37_96-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991152–54Macey1993130–37-96">
      [96]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Like his preceding work,
    <i>
     Naissance de la Clinique
    </i>
    was largely critically ignored, but later gained a cult following.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey1993140–42_95-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey1993140–42-95">
      [95]
     </a>
    </sup>
    It was of interest within the field of
    <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">
     medical ethics
    </a>
    , as it considered the ways in which the history of medicine and hospitals, and the training that those working within them receive, bring about a particular way of looking at the body: the 'medical
    <a href="/wiki/Gaze" title="Gaze">
     gaze
    </a>
    '.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
     <a href="#cite_note-97">
      [97]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault was also selected to be among the "Eighteen Man Commission" that assembled between November 1963 and March 1964 to discuss university reforms that were to be implemented by
    <a href="/wiki/Christian_Fouchet" title="Christian Fouchet">
     Christian Fouchet
    </a>
    , the Gaullist
    <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_National_Education_(France)" title="Ministry of National Education (France)">
     Minister of National Education
    </a>
    . Implemented in 1967, they brought staff strikes and student protests.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991133–36_98-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991133–36-98">
      [98]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In April 1966, Gallimard published Foucault's
    <i>
     Les Mots et les choses
    </i>
    ("Words and Things"), later translated as
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_Order_of_Things" title="The Order of Things">
      The Order of Things
     </a>
     : An Archaeology of the Human Sciences
    </i>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991155–56Macey1993159_99-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991155–56Macey1993159-99">
      [99]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Exploring how man came to be an object of knowledge, it argued that all periods of history have possessed certain underlying conditions of truth that constituted what was acceptable as scientific discourse. Foucault argues that these conditions of discourse have changed over time, from one period's
    <a href="/wiki/Episteme" title="Episteme">
     episteme
    </a>
    to another.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991158–59_100-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991158–59-100">
      [100]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Although designed for a specialist audience, the work gained media attention, becoming a surprise bestseller in France.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991155,_159Macey1993160_101-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991155,_159Macey1993160-101">
      [101]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Appearing at the height of interest in
    <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">
     structuralism
    </a>
    , Foucault was quickly grouped with scholars such as
    <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Lacan" title="Jacques Lacan">
     Jacques Lacan
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Claude_L%C3%A9vi-Strauss" title="Claude Lévi-Strauss">
     Claude Lévi-Strauss
    </a>
    , and
    <a href="/wiki/Roland_Barthes" title="Roland Barthes">
     Roland Barthes
    </a>
    , as the latest wave of thinkers set to topple the
    <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">
     existentialism
    </a>
    popularized by
    <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">
     Jean-Paul Sartre
    </a>
    . Although initially accepting this description, Foucault soon vehemently rejected it.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon19911605–162,_167_102-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon19911605–162,_167-102">
      [102]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault and Sartre regularly criticised one another in the press. Both Sartre and
    <a href="/wiki/Simone_de_Beauvoir" title="Simone de Beauvoir">
     Simone de Beauvoir
    </a>
    attacked Foucault's ideas as "
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bourgeois" title="Bourgeois">
     bourgeois
    </a>
    ", while Foucault retaliated against their Marxist beliefs by proclaiming that "Marxism exists in nineteenth-century thought as a fish exists in water; that is, it ceases to breathe anywhere else."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacey1993173–77_103-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacey1993173–77-103">
      [103]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span id="University_of_Tunis_and_Vincennes:_1966.E2.80.931970">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="University_of_Tunis_and_Vincennes:_1966–1970">
     University of Tunis and Vincennes: 1966–1970
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: University of Tunis and Vincennes: 1966–1970">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
   <blockquote class="templatequote" style="float:right; width:33%;">
    <p>
     I lived [in Tunisia] for two and a half years. It made a real impression. I was present for large, violent student riots that preceded by several weeks what happened in May in France. This was March 1968. The unrest lasted a whole year: strikes, courses suspended, arrests. And in March, a general strike by the students. The police came into the university, beat up the students, wounded several of them seriously, and started making arrests ... I have to say that I was tremendously impressed by those young men and women who took terrible risks by writing or distributing tracts or calling for strikes, the ones who really risked losing their freedom! It was a political experience for me.
    </p>
    <div class="templatequotecite">
     —
     <cite>
      Michel Foucault, 1983
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991194_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991194-104">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </cite>
    </div>
   </blockquote>
   <p>
    In September 1966, Foucault took a position teaching psychology at the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Tunis" title="University of Tunis">
     University of Tunis
    </a>
    in Tunisia. His decision to do so was largely because his lover, Defert, had been posted to the country as part of his
    <a href="/wiki/National_service" title="National service">
     national service
    </a>
    . Foucault moved a few kilometres from
    <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">
     Tunis
    </a>
    , to the village of
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidi_Bou_Sa%C3%AFd" title="Sidi Bou Saïd">
     Sidi Bou Saïd
    </a>
    , where fellow academic Gérard Deledalle lived with his wife. Soon after his arrival, Foucault announced that Tunisia was "blessed by history", a nation which "deserves to live forever because it was where
    <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">
     Hannibal
    </a>
    and
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Augustine" title="St. Augustine">
     St. Augustine
    </a>
    lived."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991187–88Macey1993145–46_105-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991187–88Macey1993145–46-105">
      [105]
     </a>
    </sup>
    His lectures at the university proved very popular, and were well attended. Although many young students were enthusiastic about his teaching, they were critical of what they believed to be his right-wing political views, viewing him as a "representative of Gaullist technocracy", even though he considered himself a leftist.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991188–89_106-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991188–89-106">
      [106]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault was in Tunis during the anti-government and pro-Palestinian riots that rocked the city in June 1967, and which continued for a year. Although highly critical of the violent, ultra-nationalistic and anti-semitic nature of many protesters, he used his status to try to prevent some of his militant leftist students from being arrested and tortured for their role in the agitation. He hid their printing press in his garden, and tried to testify on their behalf at their trials, but was prevented when the trials became closed-door events.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991192–93_107-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991192–93-107">
      [107]
     </a>
    </sup>
    While in Tunis, Foucault continued to write. Inspired by a correspondence with the surrealist artist
    <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Magritte" title="René Magritte">
     René Magritte
    </a>
    , Foucault started to write a book about the
    <a href="/wiki/Impressionism" title="Impressionism">
     impressionist
    </a>
    artist
    <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Manet" title="Édouard Manet">
     Édouard Manet
    </a>
    , but never completed it.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991190Macey1993173_108-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991190Macey1993173-108">
      [108]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In 1968, Foucault returned to Paris, moving into an apartment on the Rue de Vaugirard.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991198_109-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991198-109">
      [109]
     </a>
    </sup>
