    After the May 1968 student protests, Minister of Education
    <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Faure" title="Edgar Faure">
     Edgar Faure
    </a>
    responded by founding new universities with greater autonomy. Most prominent of these was the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_VIII" title="Paris VIII">
     Centre Expérimental de Vincennes
    </a>
    in
    <a href="/wiki/Vincennes" title="Vincennes">
     Vincennes
    </a>
    on the outskirts of Paris. A group of prominent academics were asked to select teachers to run the centre's departments, and Canguilheim recommended Foucault as head of the Philosophy Department.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991201–02_110-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991201–02-110">
      [110]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Becoming a tenured professor of Vincennes, Foucault's desire was to obtain "the best in French philosophy today" for his department, employing
    <a href="/wiki/Michel_Serres" title="Michel Serres">
     Michel Serres
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Judith_Miller_(philosopher)" title="Judith Miller (philosopher)">
     Judith Miller
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Alain_Badiou" title="Alain Badiou">
     Alain Badiou
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Ranci%C3%A8re" title="Jacques Rancière">
     Jacques Rancière
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Regnault" title="François Regnault">
     François Regnault
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Henri_Weber" title="Henri Weber">
     Henri Weber
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Balibar" title="Étienne Balibar">
     Étienne Balibar
    </a>
    , and
    <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Ch%C3%A2telet" title="François Châtelet">
     François Châtelet
    </a>
    ; most of them were Marxists or ultra-left activists.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991203–05_111-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991203–05-111">
      [111]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Lectures began at the university in January 1969, and straight away its students and staff, including Foucault, were involved in occupations and clashes with police, resulting in arrests.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991205_112-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991205-112">
      [112]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In February, Foucault gave a speech denouncing police provocation to protesters at the Latin Quarter of the Mutualité.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991206_113-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991206-113">
      [113]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Such actions marked Foucault's embrace of the ultra-left,
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991201_114-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991201-114">
      [114]
     </a>
    </sup>
    undoubtedly influenced by Defert, who had gained a job at Vincennes' sociology department and who had become a
    <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">
     Maoist
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991209_115-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991209-115">
      [115]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Most of the courses at Foucault's philosophy department were
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxism-Leninism" title="Marxism-Leninism">
     Marxist-Leninist
    </a>
    oriented, although Foucault himself gave courses on Nietzsche, "The end of Metaphysics", and "The Discourse of Sexuality", which were highly popular and over-subscribed.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991207_116-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991207-116">
      [116]
     </a>
    </sup>
    While the right-wing press was heavily critical of this new institution, new Minister of Education
    <a href="/wiki/Olivier_Guichard" title="Olivier Guichard">
     Olivier Guichard
    </a>
    was angered by its ideological bent and the lack of exams, with students being awarded degrees in a haphazard manner. He refused national accreditation of the department's degrees, resulting in a public rebuttal from Foucault.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991207–08_117-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991207–08-117">
      [117]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h2>
    <span id="Later_life_.281970.E2.80.931984.29">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_(1970–1984)">
     Later life (1970–1984)
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Later life (1970–1984)">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>
   <h3>
    <span id="Coll.C3.A8ge_de_France_and_Discipline_and_Punish:_1970.E2.80.931975">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Collège_de_France_and_Discipline_and_Punish:_1970–1975">
     Collège de France and
     <i>
      Discipline and Punish
     </i>
     : 1970–1975
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Collège de France and Discipline and Punish: 1970–1975">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Foucault desired to leave Vincennes and become a fellow of the prestigious
    <a href="/wiki/Coll%C3%A8ge_de_France" title="Collège de France">
     Collège de France
    </a>
    . He requested to join, taking up a chair in what he called the "history of systems of thought," and his request was championed by members Dumézil, Hyppolite, and Vuillemin. In November 1969, when an opening became available, Foucault was elected to the Collège, though with opposition by a large minority.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991212–18_118-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991212–18-118">
      [118]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He gave his inaugural lecture in December 1970, which was subsequently published as
    <i>
     L'Ordre du discours
    </i>
    (
    <i>
     The Discourse of Language
    </i>
    ).
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991212,_219_119-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991212,_219-119">
      [119]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He was obliged to give 12 weekly lectures a year—and did so for the rest of his life—covering the topics that he was researching at the time; these became "one of the events of Parisian intellectual life" and were repeatedly packed out events.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991222–23_120-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991222–23-120">
      [120]
     </a>
    </sup>
    On Mondays, he also gave seminars to a group of students; many of them became a "Foulcauldian tribe" who worked with him on his research. He enjoyed this teamwork and collective research, and together they published a number of short books.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991256–58_121-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991256–58-121">
      [121]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Working at the Collège allowed him to travel widely, giving lectures in Brazil, Japan, Canada, and the United States over the next 14 years.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991310_122-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991310-122">
      [122]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1970 and 1972, Foucault served as a professor in the French Department of the
    <a href="/wiki/University_at_Buffalo" title="University at Buffalo">
     University at Buffalo
    </a>
    in Buffalo, New York.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
     <a href="#cite_note-123">
      [123]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In May 1971, Foucault co-founded the Groupe d'Information sur les Prisons (GIP) along with historian
    <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Vidal-Naquet" title="Pierre Vidal-Naquet">
     Pierre Vidal-Naquet
    </a>
    and journalist
    <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marie_Domenach" title="Jean-Marie Domenach">
     Jean-Marie Domenach
    </a>
    . The GIP aimed to investigate and expose poor conditions in prisons and give prisoners and ex-prisoners a voice in French society. It was highly critical of the penal system, believing that it converted petty criminals into hardened delinquents.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991224–29_124-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991224–29-124">
      [124]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The GIP gave press conferences and staged protests surrounding the events of the Toul prison riot in December 1971, alongside other prison riots that it sparked off; in doing so it faced a police crackdown and repeated arrests.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991231–32_125-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991231–32-125">
      [125]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The group became active across France, with 2,000 to 3,000, members, but disbanded before 1974.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991233–34_126-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991233–34-126">
      [126]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Also campaigning against the death penalty, Foucault co-authored a short book on the case of the convicted murderer Pierre Rivière.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991234–35_127-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991234–35-127">
      [127]
     </a>
    </sup>
    After his research into the penal system, Foucault published
    <i>
     Surveiller et punir: Naissance de la prison
    </i>
    (
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Discipline_and_Punish" title="Discipline and Punish">
      Discipline and Punish: The Birth of the Prison
     </a>
    </i>
    ) in 1975, offering a history of the system in western Europe. In it, Foucault examines the penal evolution away from corporal and capital punishment to the penitentiary system that began in Europe and the United States around the end of the 18th century.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
     <a href="#cite_note-128">
      [128]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Biographer
    <a href="/wiki/Didier_Eribon" title="Didier Eribon">
     Didier Eribon
    </a>
    described it as "perhaps the finest" of Foucault's works, and it was well received.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991235–36_129-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991235–36-129">
      [129]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault was also active in
    <a href="/wiki/Anti-racism" title="Anti-racism">
     anti-racist
    </a>
    campaigns; in November 1971, he was a leading figure in protests following the perceived racist killing of Arab migrant Djellali Ben Ali.
    <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
     [
     <i>
      <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
       <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">
        citation needed
       </span>
      </a>
     </i>
     ]
    </sup>
    In this he worked alongside his old rival Sartre, the journalist
    <a href="/wiki/Claude_Mauriac" title="Claude Mauriac">
     Claude Mauriac
    </a>
    , and one of his literary heroes, Jean Genet. This campaign was formalised as the Committee for the Defence of the Rights of Immigrants, but there was tension at their meetings as Foucault opposed the anti-Israeli sentiment of many Arab workers and Maoist activists.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991238–42_130-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991238–42-130">
      [130]
     </a>
    </sup>
    At a December 1972 protest against the police killing of Algerian worker Mohammad Diab, both Foucault and Genet were arrested, resulting in widespread publicity.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991241–42_131-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991241–42-131">
      [131]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault was also involved in founding the Agence de Press-Libération (APL), a group of leftist journalists who intended to cover news stories neglected by the mainstream press. In 1973, they established the daily newspaper
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Lib%C3%A9ration" title="Libération">
      Libération
     </a>
    </i>
    , and Foucault suggested that they establish committees across France to collect news and distribute the paper, and advocated a column known as the "Chronicle of the Workers' Memory" to allow workers to express their opinions. Foucault wanted an active journalistic role in the paper, but this proved untenable, and he soon became disillusioned with
    <i>
     Libération
    </i>
    , believing that it distorted the facts; he did not publish in it until 1980.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991250–54_132-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991250–54-132">
      [132]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1975 he had a
    <a href="/wiki/Lysergic_acid_diethylamide" title="Lysergic acid diethylamide">
     LSD
    </a>
    experience with Simeon Wade in
    <a href="/wiki/Death_Valley" title="Death Valley">
     Death Valley
    </a>
    , California and later wrote "it was the greatest experience of his life, and that it profoundly changed his life and his work".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
     <a href="#cite_note-133">
      [133]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span id="The_History_of_Sexuality_and_Iranian_Revolution:_1976.E2.80.931979">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="The_History_of_Sexuality_and_Iranian_Revolution:_1976–1979">
     <i>
      The History of Sexuality
     </i>
     and Iranian Revolution: 1976–1979
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The History of Sexuality and Iranian Revolution: 1976–1979">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    In 1976, Gallimard published Foucault's
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">
      Histoire de la sexualité: la volonté de savoir
     </a>
    </i>
    (
    <i>
     The History of Sexuality: The Will to Knowledge
    </i>
    ), a short book exploring what Foucault called the "repressive hypothesis". It revolved largely around the concept of power, rejecting both Marxist and Freudian theory. Foucault intended it as the first in a seven-volume exploration of the subject.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991269–74_134-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991269–74-134">
      [134]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <i>
     Histoire de la sexualité
    </i>
    was a best-seller in France and gained positive press, but lukewarm intellectual interest, something that upset Foucault, who felt that many misunderstood his hypothesis.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991275–76_135-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991275–76-135">
      [135]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He soon became dissatisfied with Gallimard after being offended by senior staff member
    <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Nora" title="Pierre Nora">
     Pierre Nora
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991292_136-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991292-136">
      [136]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Along with
    <a href="/wiki/Paul_Veyne" title="Paul Veyne">
     Paul Veyne
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Wahl" title="François Wahl">
     François Wahl
    </a>
    , Foucault launched a new series of academic books, known as
    <i>
     Des travaux
    </i>
    (
    <i>
     Some Works
    </i>
    ), through the company
    <a href="/wiki/Seuil" title="Seuil">
     Seuil
    </a>
    , which he hoped would improve the state of academic research in France.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991193–95_137-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991193–95-137">
      [137]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He also produced introductions for the memoirs of
    <a href="/wiki/Herculine_Barbin" title="Herculine Barbin">
     Herculine Barbin
    </a>
    and
    <i>
     <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/My_Secret_Life_(erotica)" title="My Secret Life (erotica)">
      My Secret Life
     </a>
    </i>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991277–78_138-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991277–78-138">
      [138]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault's
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">
      Histoire de la Sexualité: la volonté de savoir
     </a>
    </i>
    concentrates on the relation between truth and sex.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_139-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-:02-139">
      [139]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He defines truth as a system of ordered procedures for the production, distribution, regulation, circulation, and operation of statements.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
     <a href="#cite_note-140">
      [140]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Through this system of truth, power structures are created and enforced. Though Foucault's definition of truth may differ from other sociologists before and after him, his work with truth in relation to power structures, such as sexuality, have left a profound mark on social science theory. In his work, he examines the heightened curiosity regarding sexuality that induced a "world of perversion" during the elite, capitalist 18th and 19th century in the western world. According to Foucault in
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">
      History of Sexuality
     </a>
    </i>
    , society of the modern age is symbolized by the conception of sexual discourses and their union with the system of truth.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_139-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-:02-139">
      [139]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In the "world of perversion", including extramarital affairs, homosexual behavior, and other such sexual promiscuities, Foucault concludes that sexual relations of the kind are constructed around producing the truth.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_141-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-:1-141">
      [141]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Sex became not only a means of pleasure, but an issue of truth.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_141-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-:1-141">
      [141]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Sex is what confines one to darkness, but also what brings one to light.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
     <a href="#cite_note-142">
      [142]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Similarly, in
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">
      The History of Sexuality
     </a>
    </i>
    , society validates and approves people based on how closely they fit the discursive mold of sexual truth.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_143-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-:2-143">
      [143]
     </a>
    </sup>
    As Foucault reminds us, in the 18th and 19th centuries, the Church was the epitome of power structure within society. Thus, many aligned their personal virtues with those of the Church, further internalizing their beliefs on the meaning of sex.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_143-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-:2-143">
      [143]
     </a>
    </sup>
    However, those who unify their sexual relation to the truth become decreasingly obliged to share their internal views with those of the Church. They will no longer see the arrangement of societal norms as an effect of the Church's deep-seated power structure.
   </p>
   <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
   <blockquote class="templatequote" style="float:right; width:33%;">
    <p>
     There exists an international citizenry that has its rights, and has its duties, and that is committed to rise up against every abuse of power, no matter who the author, no matter who the victims. After all, we are all ruled, and as such, we are in solidarity.
    </p>
    <div class="templatequotecite">
     —
     <cite>
      Michel Foucault, 1981
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199179_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199179-144">
        [144]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </cite>
    </div>
   </blockquote>
   <p>
    Foucault remained a political activist, focusing on protesting government abuses of human rights around the world. He was a key player in the 1975 protests against the Spanish government to execute 11 militants sentenced to death without fair trial. It was his idea to travel to
    <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">
     Madrid
    </a>
    with six others to give a press conference there; they were subsequently arrested and deported back to Paris.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991263–66_145-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991263–66-145">
      [145]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1977, he protested the extradition of
    <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Croissant" title="Klaus Croissant">
     Klaus Croissant
    </a>
    to West Germany, and his rib was fractured during clashes with riot police.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991280_146-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991280-146">
      [146]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In July that year, he organised an assembly of
    <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">
     Eastern Bloc
    </a>
    dissidents to mark the visit of
    <a href="/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="General Secretary of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union">
     Soviet general secretary
    </a>
    <a href="/wiki/Leonid_Brezhnev" title="Leonid Brezhnev">
     Leonid Brezhnev
    </a>
    to Paris.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991278_147-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991278-147">
      [147]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1979, he campaigned for Vietnamese political dissidents to be granted asylum in France.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991278–79_148-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991278–79-148">
      [148]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In 1977, Italian newspaper
    <i>
     <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corriere_della_sera" title="Corriere della sera">
      Corriere della sera
     </a>
    </i>
    asked Foucault to write a column for them. In doing so, in 1978 he travelled to
    <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">
     Tehran
    </a>
    in Iran, days after the
    <a href="/wiki/Black_Friday_(1978)" title="Black Friday (1978)">
     Black Friday
    </a>
    massacre. Documenting the developing
    <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">
     Iranian Revolution
    </a>
    , he met with opposition leaders such as
    <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Kazem_Shariatmadari" title="Mohammad Kazem Shariatmadari">
     Mohammad Kazem Shariatmadari
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Mehdi_Bazargan" title="Mehdi Bazargan">
     Mehdi Bazargan
    </a>
    , and discovered the popular support for
    <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">
     Islamism
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991281–85_149-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991281–85-149">
      [149]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Returning to France, he was one of the journalists who visited the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayatollah_Khomeini" title="Ayatollah Khomeini">
     Ayatollah Khomeini
    </a>
    , before visiting Tehran. His articles expressed awe of Khomeini's Islamist movement, for which he was widely criticised in the French press, including by Iranian expatriates. Foucault's response was that Islamism was to become a major political force in the region, and that the West must treat it with respect rather than hostility.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991285–88_150-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991285–88-150">
      [150]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In April 1978, Foucault traveled to Japan, where he studied
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zen_Buddhism" title="Zen Buddhism">
     Zen Buddhism
    </a>
    under
    <a href="/wiki/Omori_Sogen" title="Omori Sogen">
     Omori Sogen
    </a>
    at the Seionji temple in
    <a href="/wiki/Uenohara" title="Uenohara">
     Uenohara
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991310_122-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991310-122">
      [122]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <div class="thumb tright">
    <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
     <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_Michel_Foucault,_his_mother_and_his_father_in_Vendeuvre_du_Poitou.jpg">
      <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Graves_of_Michel_Foucault%2C_his_mother_and_his_father_in_Vendeuvre_du_Poitou.jpg/220px-Graves_of_Michel_Foucault%2C_his_mother_and_his_father_in_Vendeuvre_du_Poitou.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Graves_of_Michel_Foucault%2C_his_mother_and_his_father_in_Vendeuvre_du_Poitou.jpg/330px-Graves_of_Michel_Foucault%2C_his_mother_and_his_father_in_Vendeuvre_du_Poitou.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Graves_of_Michel_Foucault%2C_his_mother_and_his_father_in_Vendeuvre_du_Poitou.jpg/440px-Graves_of_Michel_Foucault%2C_his_mother_and_his_father_in_Vendeuvre_du_Poitou.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
     </a>
     <div class="thumbcaption">
      <div class="magnify">
       <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_Michel_Foucault,_his_mother_and_his_father_in_Vendeuvre_du_Poitou.jpg" title="Enlarge">
       </a>
      </div>
      Graves of Michel Foucault, his mother (right) and his father (left) in
      <a href="/wiki/Vendeuvre-du-Poitou" title="Vendeuvre-du-Poitou">
       Vendeuvre-du-Poitou
      </a>
     </div>
    </div>
   </div>
   <h3>
    <span id="Final_years:_1980.E2.80.931984">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years:_1980–1984">
     Final years: 1980–1984
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Final years: 1980–1984">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Although remaining critical of power relations, Foucault expressed cautious support for the
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Socialist_Party" title="French Socialist Party">
     Socialist Party
    </a>
    government of
    <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="François Mitterrand">
     François Mitterrand
    </a>
    following its
    <a href="/wiki/1981_French_presidential_election" title="1981 French presidential election">
     electoral victory in 1981
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991296–97_151-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991296–97-151">
      [151]
     </a>
    </sup>
    But his support soon deteriorated when that party refused to condemn the Polish government's crackdown on the
    <a href="/wiki/1982_demonstrations_in_Poland" title="1982 demonstrations in Poland">
     1982 demonstrations in Poland
    </a>
    orchestrated by the
    <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)">
     Solidarity
    </a>
    trade union. He and sociologist
    <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Bourdieu" title="Pierre Bourdieu">
     Pierre Bourdieu
    </a>
    authored a document condemning Mitterrand's inaction that was published in
    <i>
     Libération
    </i>
    , and they also took part in large public protests on the issue.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991298–302_152-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991298–302-152">
      [152]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault continued to support Solidarity, and with his friend
    <a href="/wiki/Simone_Signoret" title="Simone Signoret">
     Simone Signoret
    </a>
    traveled to Poland as part of a
    <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9decins_du_Monde" title="Médecins du Monde">
     Médecins du Monde
    </a>
    expedition, taking time out to visit the
    <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz_concentration_camp" title="Auschwitz concentration camp">
     Auschwitz concentration camp
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991303–03_153-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991303–03-153">
      [153]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He continued his academic research, and in June 1984 Gallimard published the second and third volumes of
    <i>
     Histoire de la sexualité
    </i>
    . Volume two,
    <i>
     L'Usage des plaisirs
    </i>
    , dealt with the "techniques of self" prescribed by ancient Greek pagan morality in relation to sexual ethics, while volume three,
    <i>
     Le Souci de soi
    </i>
    , explored the same theme in the Greek and Latin texts of the first two centuries CE. A fourth volume,
    <i>
     Les Aveux de la chair
    </i>
    , was to examine sexuality in early Christianity, but it was not finished.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991317–23_154-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991317–23-154">
      [154]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In October 1980, Foucault became a visiting professor at the University of California, Berkeley, giving the Howison Lectures on "Truth and Subjectivity", while in November he lectured at the Humanities Institute at New York University. His growing popularity in American intellectual circles was noted by
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">
      Time
     </a>
    </i>
    magazine, while Foucault went on to lecture at UCLA in 1981, the University of Vermont in 1982, and Berkeley again in 1983, where his lectures drew huge crowds.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991313–14_155-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991313–14-155">
      [155]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault spent many evenings in the San Francisco gay scene, frequenting
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sado-masochism" title="Sado-masochism">
     sado-masochistic
    </a>
    bathhouses, engaging in unprotected sex. He praised sado-masochistic activity in interviews with the gay press, describing it as "the real creation of new possibilities of pleasure, which people had no idea about previously."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991314–16Miller199326–27_156-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991314–16Miller199326–27-156">
      [156]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault contracted HIV and eventually developed AIDS. Little was known of the virus at the time; the first cases had only been identified in 1980.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199321–22_157-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199321–22-157">
      [157]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault initially referred to AIDS as a "dreamed-up disease".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
     <a href="#cite_note-158">
      [158]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In summer 1983, he developed a persistent dry cough, which concerned friends in Paris, but Foucault insisted it was just a pulmonary infection.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991324–25Miller199326_159-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991324–25Miller199326-159">
      [159]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Only when hospitalized was Foucault correctly diagnosed; treated with antibiotics, he delivered a final set of lectures at the Collège de France.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199323_160-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199323-160">
      [160]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault entered Paris' Hôpital de la
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re" title="Salpêtrière">
     Salpêtrière
    </a>
    —the same institution that he had studied in
    <i>
     Madness and Civilisation
    </i>
    —on 10 June 1984, with neurological symptoms complicated by
    <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">
     sepsis
    </a>
    . He died in the hospital on 25 June.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991357Miller199321,_24_161-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991357Miller199321,_24-161">
      [161]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h2>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Death">
     Death
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Death">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>
   <p>
    On 26 June 1984,
    <i>
     Libération
    </i>
    announced Foucault's death, mentioning the rumour that it had been brought on by AIDS. The following day,
    <i>
     Le Monde
    </i>
    issued a medical bulletin cleared by his family that made no reference to HIV/AIDS.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991327Miller199321_162-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991327Miller199321-162">
      [162]
     </a>
    </sup>
    On 29 June, Foucault's
    <i>
     la levée du corps
    </i>
    ceremony was held, in which the coffin was carried from the hospital morgue. Hundreds attended, including activists and academic friends, while
    <a href="/wiki/Gilles_Deleuze" title="Gilles Deleuze">
     Gilles Deleuze
    </a>
    gave a speech using excerpts from
    <i>
     The History of Sexuality
    </i>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991329Miller199334–36_163-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991329Miller199334–36-163">
      [163]
     </a>
    </sup>
    His body was then buried at
    <a href="/wiki/Vendeuvre-du-Poitou" title="Vendeuvre-du-Poitou">
     Vendeuvre-du-Poitou
    </a>
    in a small ceremony.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991330_164-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991330-164">
      [164]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Soon after his death, Foucault's partner
    <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defert" title="Daniel Defert">
     Daniel Defert
    </a>
    founded the first national HIV/AIDS organisation in France,
    <a href="/wiki/AIDES" title="AIDES">
     AIDES
    </a>
    ; a play on the French language word for "help" (
    <i>
     aide
    </i>
    ) and the English language acronym for the disease.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199323–24_165-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199323–24-165">
      [165]
     </a>
    </sup>
    On the second anniversary of Foucault's death, Defert publicly revealed that Foucault's death was AIDS-related in
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_Advocate_(LGBT_magazine)" title="The Advocate (LGBT magazine)">
      The Advocate
     </a>
    </i>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller199324–25_166-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller199324–25-166">
      [166]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h2>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">
     Personal life
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Personal life">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>
   <p>
    Foucault's first biographer,
    <a href="/wiki/Didier_Eribon" title="Didier Eribon">
     Didier Eribon
    </a>
    , described the philosopher as "a complex, many-sided character", and that "under one mask there is always another".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991xi_167-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991xi-167">
      [167]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He also noted that he exhibited an "enormous capacity for work".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199164_168-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199164-168">
      [168]
     </a>
    </sup>
    At the ENS, Foucault's classmates unanimously summed him up as a figure who was both "disconcerting and strange" and "a passionate worker".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199130_169-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199130-169">
      [169]
     </a>
    </sup>
    As he aged, his personality changed: Eribon noted that while he was a "tortured adolescent", post-1960, he had become "a radiant man, relaxed and cheerful", even being described by those who worked with him as a
    <a href="/wiki/Dandy" title="Dandy">
     dandy
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991138_170-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991138-170">
      [170]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He noted that in 1969, Foucault embodied the idea of "the militant intellectual".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991210_171-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991210-171">
      [171]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault was an
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist" title="Atheist">
     atheist
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
     <a href="#cite_note-172">
      [172]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
     <a href="#cite_note-173">
      [173]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He loved classical music, particularly enjoying the work of
    <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">
     Johann Sebastian Bach
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">
     Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart
    </a>
    ,
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon199183_174-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon199183-174">
      [174]
     </a>
    </sup>
    and became known for wearing
    <a href="/wiki/Polo_neck" title="Polo neck">
     turtleneck sweaters
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991311_175-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991311-175">
      [175]
     </a>
    </sup>
    After his death, Foucault's friend
    <a href="/wiki/Georges_Dum%C3%A9zil" title="Georges Dumézil">
     Georges Dumézil
    </a>
    described him as having possessed "a profound kindness and goodness", also exhibiting an "intelligence [that] literally knew no bounds."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991329_176-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991329-176">
      [176]
     </a>
    </sup>
    His life-partner
    <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defert" title="Daniel Defert">
     Daniel Defert
    </a>
    inherited his estate.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
     <a href="#cite_note-177">
      [177]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">
     Politics
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Politics">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Politically, Foucault was a leftist throughout much of his life, though his particular stance within the left often changed. In the early 1950s, while never adopting an orthodox
    <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">
     Marxist
    </a>
    viewpoint, Foucault had been a member of the
    <a href="/wiki/French_Communist_Party" title="French Communist Party">
     French Communist Party
    </a>
    , leaving the party after three years as he expressed disgust in the prejudice within its ranks against Jews and homosexuals. After spending some time working in Poland, governed at the time as a
    <a href="/wiki/Socialist_state" title="Socialist state">
     socialist state
    </a>
    by the
    <a href="/wiki/Polish_United_Workers%27_Party" title="Polish United Workers' Party">
     Polish United Workers' Party
    </a>
    , he became further disillusioned with communist ideology. As a result, in the early 1960s, Foucault was considered to be "violently anticommunist" by some of his detractors,
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991136_178-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991136-178">
      [178]
     </a>
    </sup>
    even though he was involved in leftist campaigns along with most of his students and colleagues.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
     <a href="#cite_note-179">
      [179]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Underage_sex_and_pedophilia">
     Underage sex and pedophilia
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Underage sex and pedophilia">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Foucault was a vocal proponent of
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consensual_sex" title="Consensual sex">
     consensual
    </a>
    adult-child
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Underage_sex" title="Underage sex">
     underage sex
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Pedophilia" title="Pedophilia">
     pedophilia
    </a>
    , considering them signs of liberation of both actors;
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_180-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-:0-180">
      [180]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_181-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-:3-181">
      [181]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
     <a href="#cite_note-182">
      [182]
     </a>
    </sup>
    he argued young children could give consent.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doezema_183-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Doezema-183">
      [183]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 1977, along with
    <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">
     Jean-Paul Sartre
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Derrida" title="Jacques Derrida">
     Jacques Derrida
    </a>
    , and other intellectuals, Foucault signed
    <a href="/wiki/French_petition_against_age_of_consent_laws" title="French petition against age of consent laws">
     a petition to the French parliament
    </a>
    calling for the decriminalization of all "consensual" sexual relations between adults and minors below the age of fifteen, the age of consent in France.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krizman_184-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Krizman-184">
      [184]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henley_185-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Henley-185">
      [185]
     </a>
    </sup>
    He'd also written an open letter in the
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Le_Monde" title="Le Monde">
      Le Monde
     </a>
    </i>
    in defense of three convicted pedophiles.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_180-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-:0-180">
      [180]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_181-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-:3-181">
      [181]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_186-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-:4-186">
      [186]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In the second and third volumes of Foucault’s
    <i>
     History of Sexuality
    </i>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Pederasty" title="Pederasty">
     pederasty
    </a>
    and “the possibility of the love for boys” serve a “foundational role”.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleves2018_187-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Cleves2018-187">
      [187]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schehr_188-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Schehr-188">
      [188]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault has been described by the
    <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Star" title="Windsor Star">
     Windsor Star
    </a>
    as a "pedophile guru".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
     <a href="#cite_note-189">
      [189]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/France-Am%C3%A9rique" title="France-Amérique">
      France-Amérique
     </a>
    </i>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Guy_Sorman" title="Guy Sorman">
     Guy Sorman
    </a>
    claimed that while staying in Tunisia, Foucault had practiced pedophilia by "paying for little boys".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
     <a href="#cite_note-190">
      [190]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In a further interview, he claimed Foucault would have sex with children in a graveyard.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
     <a href="#cite_note-191">
      [191]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="HIV">
     HIV
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: HIV">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Two years before his own diagnosis, Foucault expressed denial of
    <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">
     HIV
    </a>
    , suggesting an ideological motivation behind its correlation with minority groups, especially that with the
    <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">
     LGBT
    </a>
    community, of which he was a member himself.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_192-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-White-192">
      [192]
     </a>
    </sup>
    A frequent visitor of
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gay_sauna" title="Gay sauna">
     gay saunas
    </a>
    and
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%26M" title="S&amp;M">
     S&amp;M
    </a>
    clubs, Foucault's
    <a href="/wiki/Promiscuity" title="Promiscuity">
     promiscuous sexual practices
    </a>
    were criticized for imposing an overall higher risk of HIV transmission, being described as
    <a href="/wiki/Bugchasing" title="Bugchasing">
     bugchasing
    </a>
    and, more controversially,
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intentional_transmission_of_AIDS" title="Intentional transmission of AIDS">
     intentional transmission of AIDS
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehman_193-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Lehman-193">
      [193]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carvalho_194-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Carvalho-194">
      [194]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodward_195-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Woodward-195">
      [195]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h2>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_work">
     Philosophical work
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Philosophical work">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>
   <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
    See also:
    <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault_bibliography" title="Michel Foucault bibliography">
     Michel Foucault bibliography
    </a>
   </div>
   <p>
    Foucault's colleague
    <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Bourdieu" title="Pierre Bourdieu">
     Pierre Bourdieu
    </a>
    summarized the philosopher's thought as "a long exploration of transgression, of going beyond social limits, always inseparably linked to knowledge and power."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEribon1991328_196-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEribon1991328-196">
      [196]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
   <blockquote class="templatequote" style="float:right; width:33%;">
    <p>
     The theme that underlies all Foucault's work is the relationship between power and knowledge, and how the former is used to control and define the latter. What authorities claim as 'scientific knowledge' are really just means of social control. Foucault shows how, for instance, in the eighteenth century 'madness' was used to categorize and stigmatize not just the mentally ill but the poor, the sick, the homeless and, indeed, anyone whose expressions of individuality were unwelcome.
    </p>
    <div class="templatequotecite">
     —
     <cite>
      Philip Stokes,
      <i>
       Philosophy: 100 Essential Thinkers
      </i>
      (2004)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes2004187_197-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes2004187-197">
        [197]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </cite>
    </div>
   </blockquote>
   <p>
    Philosopher Philip Stokes of the
    <a href="/wiki/University_of_Reading" title="University of Reading">
     University of Reading
    </a>
    noted that overall, Foucault's work was "dark and pessimistic." Though it does, however, leave some room for optimism, in that it illustrates how the discipline of philosophy can be used to highlight areas of domination. In doing so, as Stokes claimed, the ways in which we are being dominated become better understood, so that we may strive to build social structures that minimise this risk of domination.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStokes2004187_197-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStokes2004187-197">
      [197]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In all of this development there had to be close attention to detail; it is the detail which eventually individualizes people.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall1996_198-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Marshall1996-198">
      [198]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Later in his life, Foucault explained that his work was less about analyzing power as a phenomenon than about trying to characterize the different ways in which contemporary society has expressed the use of power to "objectivise subjects." These have taken three broad forms: one involving scientific authority to classify and 'order' knowledge about human populations; the second has been to categorize and 'normalise' human subjects (by identifying madness, illness, physical features, and so on); and the third relates to the manner in which the impulse to fashion sexual identities and train one's own body to engage in routines and practices ends up reproducing certain patterns within a given society.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foucault1982_199-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Foucault1982-199">
      [199]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
     Literature
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Literature">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    In addition to his philosophical work, Foucault also wrote on literature.
    <i>
     Death and the Labyrinth: The World of Raymond Roussel,
    </i>
    published in 1963 and translated in English in 1986, is Foucault's only book-length work on literature. He described it as "by far the book I wrote most easily, with the greatest pleasure, and most rapidly."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
     <a href="#cite_note-200">
      [200]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault explores theory, criticism, and psychology with reference to the texts of
    <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Roussel" title="Raymond Roussel">
     Raymond Roussel
    </a>
    , one of the first notable experimental writers. Foucault also gave a lecture responding to Roland Barthes' famous essay "
    <a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_the_Author" title="The Death of the Author">
     The Death of the Author
    </a>
    " titled "
    <a href="/wiki/What_Is_an_Author%3F" title="What Is an Author?">
     What Is an Author?
    </a>
    " in 1969, later published in full.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
     <a href="#cite_note-201">
      [201]
     </a>
    </sup>
    According to literary theoretician Kornelije Kvas, for Foucault, "denying the existence of a historical author on account of his/ her irrelevance for interpretation is absurd, for the author is a function of the text that organizes its sense."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
     <a href="#cite_note-202">
      [202]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_power">
     Theory of power
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Theory of power">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Foucault's analysis of power comes in two forms:
    <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">
     empirical
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Theory" title="Theory">
     theoretical
    </a>
    . The empirical analyses concern themselves with historical (and modern) forms of power and how these emerged from previous forms of power. Foucault describes three types of power in his empirical analyses:
    <i>
     sovereign
    </i>
    power,
    <i>
     disciplinary
    </i>
    power, and
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Biopower" title="Biopower">
      biopower
     </a>
    </i>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
      [203]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault is generally critical of "theories" that try to give absolute answers to "everything." Therefore, he considered his own "theory" of power to be closer to a method than a typical "theory." According to Foucault, most people misunderstand power. For this reason, he makes clear that power cannot be completely described as:
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
      [203]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <ul>
    <li>
     A group of institutions and/or mechanisms whose aim it is for a citizen to obey and yield to the state (a typical liberal definition of power);
     <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-2">
      <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
       [203]
      </a>
     </sup>
    </li>
    <li>
     Yielding to rules (a typical
     <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">
      psychoanalytical
     </a>
     definition of power);
     <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-3">
      <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
       [203]
      </a>
     </sup>
     or
    </li>
    <li>
     A general and oppressing system where one societal class or group oppresses another (a typical
     <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">
      feminist
     </a>
     or
     <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Marxism" title="Orthodox Marxism">
      Orthodox Marxist
     </a>
     definition of power).
     <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-4">
      <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
       [203]
      </a>
     </sup>
    </li>
   </ul>
   <p>
    Foucault is not critical of considering these phenomena as "power", but claims that these theories of power cannot completely describe
    <i>
     all
    </i>
    forms of power. Foucault also claims that liberal definition of power has effectively hidden other forms of power to the extent that people have uncritically accepted them.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-5">
     <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
      [203]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault's own theory of power begins on micro-level, with singular "force relations".
    <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_A._Lynch&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Richard A. Lynch (page does not exist)">
     Richard A. Lynch
    </a>
    defines Foucault's concept of "force relation" as "whatever in one's social interactions that pushes, urges or compels one to do something."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
     <a href="#cite_note-204">
      [204]
     </a>
    </sup>
    According to Foucault, force relations are an effect of difference, inequality or unbalance that exists in other forms of relationships (such as sexual or economic). Force, and power, is however not something that a person or group "holds" (such as in the sovereign definition of power), instead power is a complex group of forces that comes from "everything" and therefore exists everywhere. That relations of power always result from inequality, difference or unbalance also means that power always has a goal or purpose. Power comes in two forms: tactics and strategies. Tactics is power on the micro-level, which can for example be how a person chooses to express themselves through their clothes. Strategies on the other hand, is power on macro-level, which can be the state of fashion at any moment. Strategies consist of a combination of tactics. At the same time, power is non-subjective according to Foucault. This posits a paradox, according to Lynch, since "someone" has to exert power, while at the same time there can be no "someone" exerting this power.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-6">
     <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
      [203]
     </a>
    </sup>
    According to Lynch this paradox can be solved with two observations:
   </p>
   <ul>
    <li>
     By looking at power as something which reaches further than the influence of single people or groups. Even if individuals and groups try to influence fashion, for example, their actions will often get unexpected consequences.
     <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-7">
      <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
       [203]
      </a>
     </sup>
    </li>
    <li>
     Even if individuals and groups have a free choice, they are also affected and limited by their context/situation.
     <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-8">
      <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
       [203]
      </a>
     </sup>
    </li>
   </ul>
   <p>
    According to Foucault, force relations are constantly changing, constantly interacting with other force relations which may weaken, strengthen or change one another. Foucault writes that power always includes resistance, which means there is always a possibility that power and force relations will change in some way. According to Richard A. Lynch, the purpose of Foucault's theory of power is to increase peoples' awareness of how power has shaped their way of being, thinking and acting, and by increasing this awareness making it possible for them to change their way of being, thinking and acting.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-9">
     <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
      [203]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Sovereign_power">
     Sovereign power
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Sovereign power">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    With "sovereign power" Foucault alludes to a power structure that is similar to a pyramid, where one person or a group of people (at the top of the pyramid) holds the power, while the "normal" (and oppressed) people are at the bottom of the pyramid. In the middle parts of the pyramid are the people who enforce the sovereign's orders. A typical example of sovereign power is
    <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">
     absolute monarchy
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lynch2011_203-10">
     <a href="#cite_note-Lynch2011-203">
      [203]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In historical absolute monarchies, crimes had been considered a personal offense against the sovereign and his/her power. The punishment was often public and spectacular, partly to deter others from committing crimes, but also to reinstate the sovereign's power. This was however both expensive and ineffective – it led far too often to people sympathizing with the criminal. In modern times, when disciplinary power is dominant, criminals are instead subjected to various disciplinary techniques to "remold" the criminal into a "law abiding citizen".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2011_205-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-May2011-205">
      [205]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    According to Chloë Taylor, a characteristic for sovereign power is that the sovereign has the right to take life, wealth, services, labor and products. The sovereign has a right to subtract – to take life, to enslave life, etc. – but not the right to control life in the way that later happens in disciplinary systems of power. According to Taylor, the form of power that the philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">
     Thomas Hobbes
    </a>
    is concerned about, is sovereign power. According to Hobbes, people are "free" so long they are not literally placed in chains.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011bio_206-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011bio-206">
      [206]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Disciplinary_power">
     Disciplinary power
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Disciplinary power">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    What Foucault calls "disciplinary power" aims to use bodies' skills as effectively as possible.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The more useful the body becomes, the more obedient it also has to become. The purpose of this is not only to use the bodies' skills, but also prevent these skills from being used to revolt against the power.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Disciplinary power has "individuals" as its object, target and instrument. According to Foucault, "individual" is however a construct created by disciplinary power.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The disciplinary power's techniques create a "rational self-control",
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vintges2011_208-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Vintges2011-208">
      [208]
     </a>
    </sup>
    which in practice means that the disciplinary power is internalized and therefore doesn't continuously need external force. Foucault says that disciplinary power is primarily not an oppressing form of power, but rather so a productive form of power. Disciplinary power doesn't oppress interests or desires, but instead subjects bodies to reconstructed patterns of behavior to reconstruct their thoughts, desires and interests. According to Foucault this happens in factories, schools, hospitals and prisons.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Disciplinary power creates a certain type of individual by producing new movements, habits and skills. It focuses on details, single movements, their timing and speed. It organizes bodies in time and space, and controls every movement for maximal effect. It uses rules, surveillance, exams and controls.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The activities follow certain plans, whose purpose it is to lead the bodies to certain pre-determined goals. The bodies are also combined with each other, to reach a productivity that is greater than the sum of all bodies activities.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-3">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Disciplinary power has according to Foucault been especially successful due to its usage of three technologies: hierarchical observation, normalizing judgement and exams.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-4">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
    By hierarchical observation, the bodies become constantly visible to the power. The observation is hierarchical since there is not a single observer, but rather so a "hierarchy" of observers. An example of this is
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_asylum" title="Mental asylum">
     mental asylums
    </a>
    during the 19th century, when the psychiatrist was not the only observer, but also nurses and auxiliary staff. From these observations and scientific discourses, a norm is established and used to judge the observed bodies. For the disciplinary power to continue to exist, this judgement has to be normalized. Foucault mentions several characteristics of this judgement: (1) all deviations, even small ones, from correct behavior are punished, (2) repeated rule violations are punished extra, (3) exercises are used as a behavior correcting technique and punishment, (4) rewards are used together with punishment to establish a hierarchy of good and bad behavior/people, (5) rank/grades/etc. are used as punishment and reward. Examinations combine the hierarchical observation with judgement. Exams objectify and individualize the observed bodies by creating extensive documentation about every observed body. The purpose of the exams is therefore to gather further information about each individual, track their development and compare their results to the norm.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-5">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    According to Foucault, the "formula" for disciplinary power can be seen in philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">
     Jeremy Bentham
    </a>
    's plan for the "optimal prison": the
    <a href="/wiki/Panopticon" title="Panopticon">
     panopticon
    </a>
    . Such a prison consists of a circle-formed building where every cell is inhabited by only one prisoner. In every cell there are two windows – one to let in light from outside and one pointing to the middle of the circle-formed building. In this middle there is a tower where a guard can be placed to observe the prisoners. Since the prisoners will never be able to know whether they are being watched or not at a given moment, they will internalize the disciplinary power and regulate their own behavior (
    <i>
     as if
    </i>
    they were constantly being watched). Foucault says this construction (1) creates an individuality by separating prisoners from each other in the physical room, (2) since the prisoners cannot know if they are being watched at any given moment, they internalize the disciplinary power and regulate their own behavior as if they were always watched, (3) the surveillance makes it possible to create extensive documentation about each prisoner and their behavior. According to Foucault the panopticon has been used as a model also for other disciplinary institutions, such as mental asylums in the 19th century.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-6">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h5>
    <span id="F.W._Taylor.27s_The_Principles_of_Scientific_Management">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="F.W._Taylor's_The_Principles_of_Scientific_Management">
     F.W. Taylor's
     <i>
      The Principles of Scientific Management
     </i>
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: F.W. Taylor's The Principles of Scientific Management">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h5>
   <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
    See also:
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taylorism" title="Taylorism">
     Taylorism
    </a>
   </div>
   <p>
    <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Marcelo_Hoffman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Marcelo Hoffman (page does not exist)">
     Marcelo Hoffman
    </a>
    posits that an example of disciplinary power can be seen in
    <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Winslow_Taylor" title="Frederick Winslow Taylor">
     Frederick Winslow Taylor
    </a>
    's book
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_Principles_of_Scientific_Management" title="The Principles of Scientific Management">
      The Principles of Scientific Management
     </a>
    </i>
    . Taylor's purpose was to increase the efficacy of workers by having their behavior controlled by the company's management. He mentions as an example an attempt to increase the amount of
    <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">
     pig iron
    </a>
    carried by each worker during a day from 12.5 tonnes to 47 tonnes, without causing the workers to strike. Here, Hoffman says, is a clear example of how the disciplinary power tries to make the body more obedient the more useful it becomes. Taylor describes that he started out with observing his 75 workers to pick out the most skilled workers. He had studied the workers history, character, habits and ambitions. Here is an example of how the disciplinary power creates an individuality. One of the selected workers, "Schmidt", was according to Taylor a man with high ambitions that valued a high salary. Schmidt accepted the terms given: He would earn 61% more if he agreed to obey without protest the orders given to him by an appointed instructor. Schmidt was thereafter observed and controlled in every detail of his working day – he was told when and how to work, when to rest, etc. According to Taylor, Schmidt never failed to obey during the three years during which he was subjected to this detailed control and higher workload.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-7">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Another example mentioned by Taylor is taken from a different industry, where Taylor had calculated the "optimal" workload for each worker. There Taylor had developed a system where every worker was not only continuously observed, but also punished if they had failed to reach up to the daily quota the previous workday. Every day the workers received a yellow or white note at the end of each shift, where yellow notes were given to those who had not reached the daily quota. Those who were given yellow notes were then threatened with redeployment to a "working role better fit for their productive capacities", which according to Taylor effectively led the workers to work harder. According to Taylor, the workers who were given yellow notes were not immediately redeployed. Instead, Taylor writes that a "skilled teacher" were sent to teach the workers how to do the work properly. The teachers job was however not only to "teach" the workers how to work more effectively, but also to observe them and their working capacity. Besides this teacher, Taylor also describes that the workers were observed by others, such as administrators, managers, etc.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2011_207-8">
     <a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2011-207">
      [207]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Biopower">
     Biopower
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Biopower">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
    Further information:
    <a href="/wiki/Biopower" title="Biopower">
     Biopower
    </a>
   </div>
   <p>
    With "biopower" Foucault refers to power over
    <i>
     bios
    </i>
    (life) – power over populations. Biopower primarily rests on norms which are internalized by people, rather than external force. It encourages, strengthens, controls, observes, optimizes and organize the forces below it. Foucault has sometimes described biopower as separate from disciplinary power, but at other times he has described disciplinary power as an expression of biopower. Biopower can use disciplinary techniques, but in contrast to disciplinary power its target is populations rather than individuals.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011bio_206-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011bio-206">
      [206]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Biopower studies populations regarding (for example) number of births, life expectancy, public health, housing, migration, crime, which social groups are over-represented in deviations from the norm (regarding health, crime, etc.) and tries to adjust, control or eliminate these norm deviations. One example is the age distribution in a population. Biopower is interested in age distribution to compensate for future (or current) lacks of labor power, retirement homes, etc. Yet another example is sex: because sex is connected to population growth, sex and sexuality have been of great interest to biopower. On a disciplinary level, people who engaged in non-reproductive sexual acts have been treated for psychiatric diagnoses such as "perversion", "frigidity" and "sexual dysfunction". On a biopower-level, the usage of contraceptives has been studied, some social groups have (by various means) been encouraged to have children, while others (such as poor, sick, unmarried women, criminals or people with disability) have been discouraged or prevented from having children.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011bio_206-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011bio-206">
      [206]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In the era of biopower, death has become a scandal and a catastrophe, but despite this biopower has according to Foucault killed more people than any other form of power has ever done before it. Under sovereign power, the sovereign king could kill people to exert his power or start wars simply to extend his kingdom, but during the era of biopower wars have instead been motivated by an ambition to "protect life itself". Similar motivations has also been used for genocide. For example, Nazi Germany motivated its attempt to eradicate Jews, the mentally ill and disabled with the motivation that Jews were "a threat to the German health", and that the money spent on healthcare for mentally ill and disabled would be better spent on "viable Germans". Chloë Taylor also mentions the
    <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">
     Iraq War
    </a>
    was motivated by similar tenets. The motivation was at first that Iraq was thought to have weapons of mass destruction and connections to
    <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">
     Al-Qaeda
    </a>
    . However, when the
    <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_W._Bush" title="Presidency of George W. Bush">
     Bush-
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Tony_Blair" title="Premiership of Tony Blair">
     Blair
    </a>
    -administrations didn't find any evidence to support either of these theories, the motivation for the war was changed. In the new motivation, the cause of the war was said to be that
    <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">
     Saddam Hussein
    </a>
    had committed crimes against his own population. Taylor means that in modern times, war has to be "concealed" under a rhetoric of humanitarian aid, despite the fact that these wars often cause humanitarian crises.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011bio_206-3">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011bio-206">
      [206]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    During the 19th-century, slums were increasing in number and size across the western world. Criminality, illness, alcoholism and prostitution was common in these areas, and the middle class considered the people who lived in these slums as "unmoral" and "lazy". The middle class also feared that this underclass sooner or later would "take over" because the population growth was greater in these slums than it was in the middle class. This fear gave rise to the scientific study of
    <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">
     eugenics
    </a>
    , whose founder
    <a href="/wiki/Francis_Galton" title="Francis Galton">
     Francis Galton
    </a>
    had been inspired by
    <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">
     Charles Darwin
    </a>
    and his theory of natural selection. According to Galton, society was preventing natural selection by helping "the weak", thus causing a spread of the "negative qualities" into the rest of the population.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011bio_206-4">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011bio-206">
      [206]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_the_body_and_sexuality">
     Theory of the body and sexuality
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Theory of the body and sexuality">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    According to Foucault, the body is not something objective that stands outside of history and culture. Instead, Foucault argues, the body has been and is continuously shaped by society and history – by work, diet, body ideals, exercise, medical interventions, etc. Foucault presents no "theory" of the body, but does write about it in
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Discipline_and_Punish" title="Discipline and Punish">
      Discipline and Punish
     </a>
    </i>
    as well as in
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">
      The History of Sexuality
     </a>
    </i>
