    . Foucault was critical of all purely biological explanations of phenomena such as sexuality, madness and criminality. Further, Foucault argues, that the body is not sufficient as a basis for self-understanding and understanding of others.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    In
    <i>
     Discipline and Punish
    </i>
    , Foucault shows how power and the body are tied together, for example by the disciplinary power primarily focusing on individual bodies and their behavior. Foucault argues that power, by manipulating bodies/behavior, also manipulates people's minds. Foucault turns the common saying "the body is the prison of the soul" and instead posits that "the soul is the prison of the body."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-3">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    According to Foucault,
    <a href="/wiki/Sexology" title="Sexology">
     sexology
    </a>
    has tried to exert itself as a "science" by referring to the material (the body). In contrast to this, Foucault argues that sexology is a pseudoscience, and that "sex" is a pseudo-scientific idea. For Foucault the idea of a natural, biologically grounded and fundamental sexuality is a normative historical construct that has also been used as an instrument of power. By describing sex as the biological and fundamental cause to peoples'
    <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">
     gender identity
    </a>
    , sexual identity and sexual behavior, power has effectively been able to normalize sexual and gendered behavior. This has made it possible to evaluate, pathologize and "correct" peoples' sexual and gendered behavior, by comparing bodies behaviors to the constructed "normal" behavior. For Foucault, a "normal sexuality" is as much of a construct as a "natural sexuality". Therefore, Foucault was also critical of the popular discourse that dominated the debate over sexuality during the 1960s and 1970s. During this time, the popular discourse argued for a "liberation" of sexuality from a cultural, moral and capitalistic oppression. Foucault, however, argues that peoples' opinions about and experiences of sexuality are
    <i>
     always
    </i>
    a result of cultural and power mechanisms. To "liberate" sexuality from one group of norms only means that another group of norms takes its place. This, however, does not mean that Foucault considers resistance to be futile. What Foucault argues for is rather that it is impossible to become completely free from power, and that there is simply no "natural" sexuality. Power always involves a dimension of resistance, and therefore also a possibility for change. Although Foucault considers it impossible to step outside of power-networks, it is always possible to change these networks or navigate them differently.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-4">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    According to Foucault, the body is not only an "obedient and passive object" that is dominated by discourses and power. The body is also the "seed" to resistance against dominant discourses and power techniques. The body is never fully compliant, and experiences can never fully be reduced to linguistic descriptions. There is always a possibility to experience something that is not possible to describe with words, and in this discrepancy there is also a possibility for resistance against dominant discourses.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-5">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault's view of the historical construction of the body has influenced many feminist and queer-theorists. According to
    <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Johanna_Oksala&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Johanna Oksala (page does not exist)">
     Johanna Oksala
    </a>
    , Foucault's influence on queer theory has been so great than he can be considered one of the founders of queer theory. The fundamental idea behind queer theory is that there is no natural fundament that lies behind identities such as gay, lesbian, heterosexual, etc. Instead these identities are considered cultural constructions that have been constructed through normative discourses and relations of power. Feminists have with the help of Foucault's ideas studied different ways that women form their bodies: through plastic surgery, diet, eating disorders, etc. Foucault's historization of sex has also affected feminist theorists such as
    <a href="/wiki/Judith_Butler" title="Judith Butler">
     Judith Butler
    </a>
    , who used Foucault's theories about the relation between subject, power and sex to question gendered subjects. Butler follows Foucault by saying that there is no "true" gender behind gender identity that constitutes its biological and objective fundament. However, Butler is critical of Foucault. She argues Foucault "naively" presents bodies and pleasures as a ground for resistance against power, without extending his historization of sexuality to gendered subjects/bodies. Foucault has received criticism from other feminists, such as
    <a href="/wiki/Susan_Bordo" title="Susan Bordo">
     Susan Bordo
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Kate_Soper" title="Kate Soper">
     Kate Soper
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-6">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Johanna Oksala argues that Foucault, by saying that sex/sexuality are constructed, doesn't deny the existence of sexuality. Oksala also argues that the goal of critical theories such as Foucault is not to liberate the body and sexuality from oppression, but rather to question and deny the identities that are posited as "natural" and "essential" by showing how these identities are historical and cultural constructions.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-7">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_subjectivity">
     Theory of subjectivity
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Theory of subjectivity">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Foucault considered his primary project to be the investigation of how people through history have been made into "subjects."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011intro_210-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011intro-210">
      [210]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Subjectivity, for Foucault, is not a state of being, but a practice – an active "being."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011practices_211-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011practices-211">
      [211]
     </a>
    </sup>
    According to Foucault, "the subject" has, by western philosophers, usually been considered as something given; natural and objective. On the contrary, Foucault considers subjectivity to be a construction created by power.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011intro_210-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011intro-210">
      [210]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault talks of "assujettissement", which is a French term that for Foucault refers to a process where power creates subjects while also oppressing them using social norms. For Foucault "social norms" are standards that people are encouraged to follow, that are also used to compare and define people. As an example of "assujettissement", Foucault mentions "homosexual", a historically contingent type of subjectivity that was created by sexology. Foucault writes that
    <a href="/wiki/Sodomy" title="Sodomy">
     sodomy
    </a>
    was previously considered a serious sexual deviation, but a temporary one. Homosexuality, however, became a "species", a past, a childhood and a type of life. "Homosexuals" has by the same power that created this subjectivity been discriminated against, due to homosexuality being considered as a deviation from the "normal" sexuality. However, Foucault argues, the creation of a subjectivity such as "homosexuality" does not only have negative consequences for the people who are subjectivised – the subjectivity of homosexuality has also led to the creation of gay bars and the
    <a href="/wiki/Pride_parade" title="Pride parade">
     pride parade
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heyes2011_212-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Heyes2011-212">
      [212]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    According to Foucault, scientific discourses have played an important role in the disciplinary power system, by classifying and categorizing people, observing their behavior and "treating" them when their behavior has been considered "abnormal". He defines discourse as a form of
    <a href="/wiki/Oppression" title="Oppression">
     oppression
    </a>
    that does not require physical force. He identifies its production as "controlled, selected, organized and redistributed by a certain number of procedures", which are driven by individuals' aspiration of knowledge to create "rules" and "systems" that translate into social codes.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213">
     <a href="#cite_note-213">
      [213]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Moreover, discourse creates a force that extends beyond societal institutions and could be found in social and formal fields such as health care systems, educational and law enforcement. The formation of these fields may seem to contribute to social development; however, Foucault warns against discourses' harmful aspects on society.
   </p>
   <p>
    Sciences such as psychiatry, biology, medicine, economy, psychoanalysis, psychology, sociology, ethnology, pedagogy and criminology have all categorized behaviors as rational, irrational, normal, abnormal, human, inhuman, etc. By doing so, they have all created various types of subjectivity and norms,
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vintges2011_208-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Vintges2011-208">
      [208]
     </a>
    </sup>
    which are then internalized by people as "truths". People have then adapted their behavior to get closer to what these sciences has labeled as "normal".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksala2011_209-8">
     <a href="#cite_note-Oksala2011-209">
      [209]
     </a>
    </sup>
    For example, Foucault claims that psychological observation/surveillance and psychological discourses has created a type of psychology-centered subjectivity, which has led to people considering unhappiness a fault in their psychology rather than in society. This has also, according to Foucault, been a way for society to resist criticism – criticism against society has been turned against the individual and their psychological health.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2011_205-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-May2011-205">
      [205]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Self-constituting_subjectivity">
     Self-constituting subjectivity
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Self-constituting subjectivity">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    According to Foucault, subjectivity is not necessarily something that is forced upon people externally – it is also something that is established in a person's relation to themselves.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011practices_211-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011practices-211">
      [211]
     </a>
    </sup>
    This can, for example, happen when a person is trying to "find themselves" or "be themselves", something Edward McGushin describes as a typical modern activity. In this quest for the "true self", the self is established in two levels: as a passive object (the "true self" that is searched for) and as an active "searcher". The ancient
    <a href="/wiki/Cynicism_(philosophy)" title="Cynicism (philosophy)">
     Cynics
    </a>
    and the 19th-century philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">
     Friedrich Nietzsche
    </a>
    posited that the "true self" can only be found by going through great hardship and/or danger. The ancient
    <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">
     Stoics
    </a>
    and 17th-century philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">
     René Descartes
    </a>
    , however, argued that the "self" can be found by quiet and solitary introspection. Yet another example is
    <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">
     Socrates
    </a>
    , who argued that self-awareness can only be found by having debates with others, where the debaters question each other's foundational views and opinions. Foucault, however, argued that "subjectivity" is a process, rather than a state of being. As such, Foucault argued that there is no "true self" to be found. Rather so, the "self" is constituted/created in activities such as the ones employed to "find" the "self". In other words, exposing oneself to hardships and danger does not "reveal" the "true self", according to Foucault, but rather creates a particular type of self and subjectivity. However, according to Foucault the "form" for the subject is in great part already constituted by power, before these self-constituting practices are employed. Schools, workplaces, households, government institutions, entertainment media and the healthcare sector all, through disciplinary power, contribute to forming people into being particular types of subjects.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGushin2011_214-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-McGushin2011-214">
      [214]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_freedom">
     Theory of freedom
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Theory of freedom">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <p>
    Todd May defines Foucault's concept of freedom as: that which we can do of ourselves within our specific historical context. A condition for this, according to Foucault, is that we are aware of our situation and how it has been created/affected (and is still being affected) by power. According to May, two of the aspects of how power has shaped peoples′ way of being, thinking and acting is described in the books where Foucault describes disciplinary power and the history of sexuality. However, May argues, there will always be aspects of peoples′ formation that will be unknown to them, hence the constant necessity for the type of analyses that Foucault did.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2011_205-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-May2011-205">
      [205]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault argues that the forces that have affected people can be changed; people always have the capacity to change the factors that limit their freedom.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2011_205-3">
     <a href="#cite_note-May2011-205">
      [205]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Freedom is thus not a state of being, but a practice – a way of being in relation to oneself, to others and to the world.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mendieta2011_215-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Mendieta2011-215">
      [215]
     </a>
    </sup>
    According to Todd May Foucault's concept of freedom also includes constructing histories like the ones Foucault did about the history of disciplinary power and sexuality – histories that investigate and describe the forces that have influenced people into becoming who they are. From the knowledge that is reached from such investigations, people can thereafter decide which forces they believe are acceptable and which they consider to be intolerable and has to be changed. Freedom is for Foucault a type of "experimentation" with different "transformations". Since these experiments cannot be controlled completely, May argues they may lead to the reconstruction of intolerable power relations or the creation of new ones. Thus, May argues, it is always necessary to continue with such experimentation and Foucauldian analyses.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-May2011_205-4">
     <a href="#cite_note-May2011-205">
      [205]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Practice_of_critique">
     Practice of critique
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Practice of critique">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    Foucault's "alternative" to the modern subjectivity is described by Cressida Heyes as "critique." For Foucault there are no "good" and "bad" forms of subjectivity, since they are all a result of power relations.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heyes2011_212-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Heyes2011-212">
      [212]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In the same way, Foucault argues there are no "good" and "bad" norms. All norms and institutions are at the same time enabling as they are oppressing. Therefore, Foucault argues, it is always crucial to continue with the practice of "critique".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011practices_211-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011practices-211">
      [211]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Critique is for Foucault a practice that searches for the processes and events that led to our way of being – a questioning of who we "are" and how this "we" came to be. Such a "critical
    <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">
     ontology
    </a>
    of the present" shows that peoples′ current "being" is in fact a historically contingent, unstable and changeable construction. Foucault emphasizes that since the current way of being is not a necessity, it is also possible to change it.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mendieta2011_215-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Mendieta2011-215">
      [215]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Critique also includes investigating how and when people are being enabled and when they are being oppressed by the current norms and institutions, finding ways to reduce limitations on freedom, resist normalization and develop new and different way of relating to oneself and others. Foucault argues that it is impossible to go beyond power relations, but that it is always possible to navigate power relations in a different way.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011practices_211-3">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011practices-211">
      [211]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span id="Epimeleia_heautou.2C_.22care_for_the_self.22">
    </span>
    <span class="mw-headline" id='Epimeleia_heautou,_"care_for_the_self"'>
     <span class="anchor" id="epimeleia_heautou">
     </span>
     <i>
      Epimeleia heautou
     </i>
     , "care for the self"
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title='Edit section: Epimeleia heautou, "care for the self"'>
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    As an alternative to the modern "search" for the "true self",
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGushin2011_214-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-McGushin2011-214">
      [214]
     </a>
    </sup>
    and as a part of "the work of freedom",
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mendieta2011_215-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-Mendieta2011-215">
      [215]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault discusses the antique Greek term
    <i>
     <b>
      epimeleia heautou
     </b>
    </i>
    , "care for the self" (ἐπιμέλεια ἑαυτοῦ). According to Foucault, among the ancient Greek philosophers, self-awareness was not a goal in itself, but rather something that was sought after in order to "care for oneself". Care for the self consists of what Foucault calls "the art of living" or "technologies of the self."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGushin2011_214-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-McGushin2011-214">
      [214]
     </a>
    </sup>
    The goal of these techniques was, according to Foucault, to transform oneself into a more ethical person. As an example of this, Foucault mentions
    <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">
     meditation
    </a>
    ,
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vintges2011_208-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-Vintges2011-208">
      [208]
     </a>
    </sup>
    the
    <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">
     stoic
    </a>
    activity of contemplating past and future actions and evaluating if these actions are in line with one's values and goals, and "contemplation of nature." Contemplation of nature is another stoic activity, that consists of reflecting on how "small" one's existence is when compared to the greater
    <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">
     cosmos
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGushin2011_214-3">
     <a href="#cite_note-McGushin2011-214">
      [214]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_knowledge">
     Theory of knowledge
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Theory of knowledge">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
    Main article:
    <a href="/wiki/Power-knowledge" title="Power-knowledge">
     Power-knowledge
    </a>
   </div>
   <p>
    Foucault is described by Mary Beth Mader as an
    <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">
     epistemological
    </a>
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constructivist_epistemology" title="Constructivist epistemology">
     constructivist
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Historicism" title="Historicism">
     historicist
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mader2014_216-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Mader2014-216">
      [216]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault is critical of the idea that humans can reach "absolute" knowledge about the world. A fundamental goal in many of Foucault's works is to show how that which has traditionally been considered as absolute, universal and true in fact are historically contingent. To Foucault, even the idea of absolute knowledge is a historically contingent idea. This does however not lead to epistemological nihilism; rather, Foucault argues that we "always begin anew" when it comes to knowledge.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011intro_210-2">
     <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011intro-210">
      [210]
     </a>
    </sup>
    At the same time Foucault is critical of modern western philosophy for lacking "spirituality". With "spirituality" Foucault refers to a certain type of ethical being, and the processes that lead to this state of being. Foucault argues that such a spirituality was a natural part of the ancient Greek philosophy, where knowledge was considered as something that was only accessible to those that had an ethical character. According to Foucault this changed in the "cartesian moment", the moment when
    <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">
     René Descartes
    </a>
    reached the "insight" that self-awareness was something given (
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cogito_ergo_sum" title="Cogito ergo sum">
     Cogito ergo sum
    </a>
    , "I think, therefore I am"), and from this "insight" Descartes drew conclusions about God, the world, and knowledge. According to Foucault, since Descartes knowledge has been something separate from ethics. In modern times, Foucault argues, anyone can reach "knowledge", as long as they are rational beings, educated, willing to participate in the scientific community and use a scientific method. Foucault is critical of this "modern" view of knowledge.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
     <a href="#cite_note-217">
      [217]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault describes two types of "knowledge": "savoir" and "connaissance", two French terms that both can be translated as "knowledge" but with separate meanings for Foucault. By "savoir" Foucault is referring to a process where subjects are created, while at the same time these subjects also become objects for knowledge. An example of this can be seen in criminology and psychiatry. In these sciences, subjects such as "the rational person", "the mentally ill person", "the law abiding person", "the criminal", etc. are created, and these sciences center their attention and knowledge on these subjects. The knowledge about these subjects is "connaissance", while the process in which subjects and knowledge is created is "savoir".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mader2014_216-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Mader2014-216">
      [216]
     </a>
    </sup>
    A similar term in Foucaults corpus is "pouvoir/savoir" (power/knowledge). With this term Foucault is referring to a type of knowledge that is considered "common sense", but that is created and withheld in that position (as "common sense") by power. The term power/knowledge comes from
    <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">
     Jeremy Bentham
    </a>
    's idea that
    <a href="/wiki/Panopticon" title="Panopticon">
     panopticons
    </a>
    wouldn't only be prisons, but would be used for experiments where the criminals′ behaviour would be studied. Power/knowledge thus refers to forms of power where the power compares individuals, measures differences, establishes a norm and then forces this norm unto the subjects. This is especially successful when the established norm is internalized and institutionalized (by "institutionalized" Foucault refers to when the norm is omnipresent). Because then, when the norm is internalized and institutionalized, it has effectively become a part of peoples' "common sense" – the "obvious", the "given", the "natural". When this has happened, this "common sense" also affects the explicit knowledge (scientific knowledge), Foucault argues. Ellen K. Feder states that the premise "the world consists of women and men" is an example of this. This premise, Feder argues, has been considered "common sense", and has led to the creation of the psychiatric diagnosis
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_identity_disorder" title="Gender identity disorder">
     gender identity disorder
    </a>
    (GID). For example, during the 1970s, children with behavior that was not considered appropriate for their gender was diagnosed with GID. The treatment then consisted of trying to make the child adapt to the prevailing gender norms. Feder argues that this is an example of power/knowledge since psychiatry, from the "common sense" premise "the world consists of women and men" (a premise which is upheld in this status by power), created a new diagnosis, a new type of subject and a whole body of knowledge surrounding this new subject.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feder2011_218-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Feder2011-218">
      [218]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h2>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_and_reception">
     Influence and reception
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Influence and reception">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>
   <p>
    Foucault's works have exercised a powerful influence over numerous humanistic and social scientific disciplines as one of the most influential and controversial scholars of the post-World War II period.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gutting1_219-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-gutting1-219">
      [219]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220">
     <a href="#cite_note-220">
      [220]
     </a>
    </sup>
    According to a London School of Economics' analysis in 2016, his works
    <i>
     Discipline and Punish
    </i>
    and
    <i>
     The History of Sexuality
    </i>
    were among the 25 most cited books in the social sciences of all time, at just over 100,000 citations.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221">
     <a href="#cite_note-221">
      [221]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In 2007, Foucault was listed as the single most cited scholar in the humanities by the
    <i>
     <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISI_Web_of_Science" title="ISI Web of Science">
      ISI Web of Science
     </a>
    </i>
    among a large quantity of French philosophers, the compilation's author commenting that "What this says of modern scholarship is for the reader to decide—and it is imagined that judgments will vary from admiration to despair, depending on one's view".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222">
     <a href="#cite_note-222">
      [222]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    According to
    <a href="/wiki/Gary_Gutting" title="Gary Gutting">
     Gary Gutting
    </a>
    , Foucault's "detailed historical remarks on the emergence of disciplinary and regulatory
    <a href="/wiki/Biopower" title="Biopower">
     biopower
    </a>
    have been widely influential."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223">
     <a href="#cite_note-223">
      [223]
     </a>
    </sup>
    <a href="/wiki/Leo_Bersani" title="Leo Bersani">
     Leo Bersani
    </a>
    wrote that:
   </p>
   <blockquote>
    <p>
     "[Foucault] is our most brilliant philosopher of power. More originally than any other contemporary thinker, he has attempted to define the historical constraints under which we live, at the same time that he has been anxious to account for—if possible, even to locate—the points at which we might resist those constraints and counter some of the moves of power. In the present climate of cynical disgust with the exercise of political power, Foucault's importance can hardly be exaggerated."
     <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224">
      <a href="#cite_note-224">
       [224]
      </a>
     </sup>
    </p>
   </blockquote>
   <p>
    Foucault's work on "biopower" has been widely influential within the disciplines of philosophy and
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_theory" title="Political theory">
     political theory
    </a>
    , particularly for such authors as
    <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Agamben" title="Giorgio Agamben">
     Giorgio Agamben
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Roberto_Esposito" title="Roberto Esposito">
     Roberto Esposito
    </a>
    ,
    <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Negri" title="Antonio Negri">
     Antonio Negri
    </a>
    , and
    <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hardt" title="Michael Hardt">
     Michael Hardt
    </a>
    .
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225">
     <a href="#cite_note-225">
      [225]
     </a>
    </sup>
    His discussions on power and discourse have inspired many
    <a href="/wiki/Critical_theory" title="Critical theory">
     critical theorists
    </a>
    , who believe that Foucault's analysis of power structures could aid the struggle against inequality. They claim that through
    <a href="/wiki/Discourse_analysis" title="Discourse analysis">
     discourse analysis
    </a>
    , hierarchies may be uncovered and questioned by way of analyzing the corresponding fields of knowledge through which they are legitimated. This is one of the ways that Foucault's work is linked to critical theory.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226">
     <a href="#cite_note-226">
      [226]
     </a>
    </sup>
    His work
    <i>
     Discipline and Punish
    </i>
    influenced his friend and contemporary
    <a href="/wiki/Gilles_Deleuze" title="Gilles Deleuze">
     Gilles Deleuze
    </a>
    , who published the paper "Postscript on the Societies of Control", praising Foucault's work but arguing that contemporary western society has in fact developed from a 'disciplinary society' into a 'society of control'.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227">
     <a href="#cite_note-227">
      [227]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Deleuze went on to publish a book dedicated to Foucault's thought in 1988 under the title
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Foucault_(Deleuze_book)" title="Foucault (Deleuze book)">
      Foucault
     </a>
    </i>
    .
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault's discussions of the relationship between power and knowledge has influenced postcolonial critiques in explaining the discursive formation of
    <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">
     colonialism
    </a>
    , particularly in
    <a href="/wiki/Edward_Said" title="Edward Said">
     Edward Said
    </a>
    's work
    <i>
     <a href="/wiki/Orientalism_(book)" title="Orientalism (book)">
      Orientalism
     </a>
     .
    </i>
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228">
     <a href="#cite_note-228">
      [228]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault's work has been compared to that of
    <a href="/wiki/Erving_Goffman" title="Erving Goffman">
     Erving Goffman
    </a>
    by the sociologist
    <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hviid_Jacobsen" title="Michael Hviid Jacobsen">
     Michael Hviid Jacobsen
    </a>
    and Soren Kristiansen, who list Goffman as an influence on Foucault.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229">
     <a href="#cite_note-229">
      [229]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Foucault's writings, particularly
    <i>
     The History of Sexuality
    </i>
    , have also been very influential in
    <a href="/wiki/Feminist_philosophy" title="Feminist philosophy">
     feminist philosophy
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Queer_theory" title="Queer theory">
     queer theory
    </a>
    , particularly the work of the major Feminist scholar
    <a href="/wiki/Judith_Butler" title="Judith Butler">
     Judith Butler
    </a>
    due to his theories regarding the genealogy of maleness and femaleness, power, sexuality, and bodies.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gutting1_219-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-gutting1-219">
      [219]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h3>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Critiques_and_engagements">
     Critiques and engagements
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Critiques and engagements">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h3>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Crypto-normativity">
     Crypto-normativity
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Crypto-normativity">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
    Main article:
    <a href="/wiki/Foucault%E2%80%93Habermas_debate" title="Foucault–Habermas debate">
     Foucault–Habermas debate
    </a>
   </div>
   <p>
    A prominent critique of Foucault's thought concerns his refusal to propose positive solutions to the social and political issues that he critiques. Since no human relation is devoid of power, freedom becomes elusive—even as an ideal. This stance which critiques normativity as socially constructed and contingent, but which relies on an implicit norm to mount the critique led philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Habermas" title="Jürgen Habermas">
     Jürgen Habermas
    </a>
    to describe Foucault's thinking as  "crypto-normativist", covertly reliant on the very
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_enlightenment" title="Age of enlightenment">
     Enlightenment
    </a>
    principles he attempts to argue against.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230">
     <a href="#cite_note-230">
      [230]
     </a>
    </sup>
    A similar critique has been advanced by
    <a href="/wiki/Diana_Taylor_(professor)" title="Diana Taylor (professor)">
     Diana Taylor
    </a>
    , and by
    <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Fraser" title="Nancy Fraser">
     Nancy Fraser
    </a>
    who argues that "Foucault's critique encompasses traditional moral systems, he denies himself recourse to concepts such as 'freedom' and 'justice', and therefore lacks the ability to generate positive alternatives."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231">
     <a href="#cite_note-231">
      [231]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Genealogy_as_historical_method">
     Genealogy as historical method
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Genealogy as historical method">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    The philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rorty" title="Richard Rorty">
     Richard Rorty
    </a>
    has argued that Foucault's "archaeology of knowledge" is fundamentally negative, and thus fails to adequately establish any "new" theory of knowledge
    <i>
     per se
    </i>
    . Rather, Foucault simply provides a few valuable maxims regarding the reading of history. Rorty writes:
   </p>
   <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
   <blockquote class="templatequote">
    <p>
     As far as I can see, all he has to offer are brilliant redescriptions of the past, supplemented by helpful hints on how to avoid being trapped by old historiographical assumptions. These hints consist largely of saying: "do not look for progress or meaning in history; do not see the history of a given activity, of any segment of culture, as the development of rationality or of freedom; do not use any philosophical vocabulary to characterize the essence of such activity or the goal it serves; do not assume that the way this activity is presently conducted gives any clue to the goals it served in the past".
     <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232">
      <a href="#cite_note-232">
       [232]
      </a>
     </sup>
    </p>
   </blockquote>
   <p>
    Foucault has frequently been criticized by historians for what they consider to be a lack of rigor in his analyses.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233">
     <a href="#cite_note-233">
      [233]
     </a>
    </sup>
    For example,
    <a href="/wiki/Hans-Ulrich_Wehler" title="Hans-Ulrich Wehler">
     Hans-Ulrich Wehler
    </a>
    harshly criticized Foucault in 1998.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234">
     <a href="#cite_note-234">
      [234]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Wehler regards Foucault as a bad philosopher who wrongfully received a good response by the humanities and by social sciences. According to Wehler, Foucault's works are not only insufficient in their empiric historical aspects, but also often contradictory and lacking in clarity. For example, Foucault's concept of power is "desperatingly undifferentiated", and Foucault's thesis of a "disciplinary society" is, according to Wehler, only possible because Foucault does not properly differentiate between authority, force, power, violence and legitimacy.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235">
     <a href="#cite_note-235">
      [235]
     </a>
    </sup>
    In addition, his thesis is based on a one-sided choice of sources (prisons and psychiatric institutions) and neglects other types of organizations as e.g. factories. Also, Wehler criticizes Foucault's "francocentrism" because he did not take into consideration major German-speaking theorists of social sciences like
    <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">
     Max Weber
    </a>
    and
    <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Elias" title="Norbert Elias">
     Norbert Elias
    </a>
    . In all, Wehler concludes that Foucault is "because of the endless series of flaws in his so-called empirical studies ... an intellectually dishonest, empirically absolutely unreliable, crypto-normativist seducer of Postmodernism".
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236">
     <a href="#cite_note-236">
      [236]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Feminist_critiques">
     Feminist critiques
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Feminist critiques">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    Though American feminists have built on Foucault's critiques of the historical construction of gender roles and sexuality, some feminists note the limitations of the masculinist subjectivity and ethical orientation that he describes.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Downing,_Lisa_2008_237-0">
     <a href="#cite_note-Downing,_Lisa_2008-237">
      [237]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Sexuality">
     Sexuality
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Sexuality">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    The philosopher
    <a href="/wiki/Roger_Scruton" title="Roger Scruton">
     Roger Scruton
    </a>
    argues in
    <i>
     <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sexual_Desire_(book)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sexual Desire (book) (page does not exist)">
      Sexual Desire
     </a>
    </i>
    (1986) that Foucault was incorrect to claim, in
    <i>
     The History of Sexuality
    </i>
    , that sexual morality is culturally relative. He criticizes Foucault for assuming that there could be societies in which a "problematisation" of the sexual did not occur, concluding that, "No history of thought could show the 'problematisation' of sexual experience to be peculiar to certain specific social formations: it is characteristic of personal experience generally, and therefore of every genuine social order."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238">
     <a href="#cite_note-238">
      [238]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    Foucault's approach to sexuality, which he sees as socially constructed, has become influential in
    <a href="/wiki/Queer_theory" title="Queer theory">
     queer theory
    </a>
    . Foucault's resistance to identity politics, and his rejection of the psychoanalytic concept of "object choice", stands at odds with some theories of queer identity.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Downing,_Lisa_2008_237-1">
     <a href="#cite_note-Downing,_Lisa_2008-237">
      [237]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Social_constructionism_and_human_nature">
     Social constructionism and human nature
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Social constructionism and human nature">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    Foucault is sometimes criticized for his prominent formulation of principles of
    <a href="/wiki/Social_constructionism" title="Social constructionism">
     social constructionism
    </a>
    , which some see as an affront to the concept of
    <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">
     truth
    </a>
    . In Foucault's 1971
    <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chomsky-Foucault_Debate" title="Chomsky-Foucault Debate">
     televised debate
    </a>
    with
    <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">
     Noam Chomsky
    </a>
    , Foucault argued against the possibility of any fixed human nature, as posited by Chomsky's concept of innate human faculties. Chomsky argued that concepts of justice were rooted in human reason, whereas Foucault rejected the universal basis for a concept of justice.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239">
     <a href="#cite_note-239">
      [239]
     </a>
    </sup>
    Following the debate, Chomsky was stricken with Foucault's total rejection of the possibility of a universal morality, stating "He struck me as completely amoral, I'd never met anyone who was so totally amoral [...] I mean, I liked him personally, it's just that I couldn't make sense of him. It's as if he was from a different species, or something."
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240">
     <a href="#cite_note-240">
      [240]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h4>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_authority">
     Education and authority
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Education and authority">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h4>
   <p>
    Peruvian writer
    <a href="/wiki/Mario_Vargas_Llosa" title="Mario Vargas Llosa">
     Mario Vargas Llosa
    </a>
    , while acknowledging that Foucault contributed to give a right of citizenship in cultural life to certain marginal and eccentric experiences (of sexuality, of cultural repression, of madness), asserts that his radical critique of authority was detrimental to education.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241">
     <a href="#cite_note-241">
      [241]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <p>
    <b>
     Psychology of the self
    </b>
   </p>
   <p>
    One of Foucault's claims regarding the subjectivity of the self has been disputed. Opposing Foucault's view of subjectivity, it is possibly more reasonable to assume that other factors, such as biological, environmental, and cultural are explanations for the self.
    <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242">
     <a href="#cite_note-242">
      [242]
     </a>
    </sup>
   </p>
   <h2>
    <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
     References
    </span>
    <span class="mw-editsection">
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      [
     </span>
     <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michel_Foucault&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: References">
      edit
     </a>
     <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
      ]
     </span>
    </span>
   </h2>

   </style>
   <div class="reflist">

       <span class="reference-text">
        <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
        <cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFWillsher2020">
         Willsher, Kim (21 February 2020).
         <a class="external text" href="https://www.latimes.com/world-nation/story/2020-02-21/french-writer-gabriel-matzneff-pedophile" rel="nofollow">
          "This French writer operated openly as a pedophile for decades. He may finally face justice"
         </a>
         .
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">
           Los Angeles Times
          </a>
         </i>

         <q>
          He references a moment when famous writers including Michel Foucault, Jean-Paul Sartre and Simone de Beauvoir and respected newspapers, notably Libération and Le Monde, defended the idea that sex with minors was a form of liberation for both parties.
         </q>

          Under this interpretation of liberté, young children were empowered to find happiness in sexual relationships; their ability to consent was a foregone conclusion.        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

         <q>
          I hate to say it but it's not in the least bit surprising that this is a French film. In the 1970s, key thinkers of the French intelligentsia — Michel Foucault, Roland Barthes, Simone de Beauvoir, and Jean-Paul Satre among them — published an open letter in Le Monde, a leading French newspaper, defending three men who had been charged with having sex with children under 15.

        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=National+Review&amp;rft.atitle=The+Problem+with+Cuties&amp;rft.date=2020-09-13&amp;rft.aulast=Kearns&amp;rft.aufirst=Madeleine&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nationalreview.com%2F2020%2F09%2Fthe-problem-with-cuties%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMichel+Foucault">

       <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
        <b>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Cleves2018_187-0">
          ^
         </a>
        </b>
       </span>
       <span class="reference-text">
        <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
        <cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFCleves2018">
         <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Hope_Cleves" title="Rachel Hope Cleves">
          Cleves, Rachel Hope
         </a>
         (2018). "From pederasty to pedophilia: Sex between children or youth and adults in U.S. history".
         <i>
          History Compass
         </i>
         .
         <b>
          16
         </b>
         (1): e12435.
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">
          doi
         </a>
         :
         <a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fhic3.12435" rel="nofollow">
          10.1111/hic3.12435
         </a>
         .
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">
          ISSN
         </a>
         <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1478-0542" rel="nofollow">

         </a>

         <q>
          The example of Ancient Greek pederasty also served a foundational role in Michel Foucault's History of Sexuality, which provides the theoretical basis for most of the historiography of American sexuality. Foucault conceptualized the History of Sexuality as a multi‐volume series that would extend from the Ancient Greeks through modern times, although he died before he could complete the work. The first and best‐known volume in the series introduced the project. Volume 2, The Use of Pleasure, examined Ancient Greece. Foucault sought to understand not simply why “the Greeks practiced, accepted, and valued relations between men and boys” but why they crafted a system of ethics that regulated this sexual practice. According to Foucault, “what is historically singular is not that the Greeks found pleasure in boys, nor even that they accepted this pleasure as legitimate; it is that this acceptance of pleasure was not simple, and that it gave rise to a whole cultural elaboration”.
         </q>
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=History+Compass&amp;rft.atitle=From+pederasty+to+pedophilia%3A+Sex+between+children+or+youth+and+adults+in+U.S.+history&amp;rft.volume=16&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=e12435&amp;rft.date=2018&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fhic3.12435&amp;rft.issn=14780542&amp;rft.aulast=Cleves&amp;rft.aufirst=Rachel+Hope&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMichel+Foucault">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
      <li id="cite_note-Schehr-188">
       <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
        <b>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Schehr_188-0">
          ^
         </a>
        </b>
       </span>
       <span class="reference-text">
        <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
        <cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFSchehr1994">
         Schehr, Lawrence R. (March 1994).
         <a class="external text" href="https://www.pdcnet.org/jfp/content/jfp_1994_0006_0001_0059_0075" rel="nofollow">
          "Foucault's Body"
         </a>
         .
         <i>
          Journal of French and Francophone Philosophy
         </i>
         .
         <b>
          6
         </b>
         (1–2): 59–75
         <span class="reference-accessdate">
          . Retrieved
          <span class="nowrap">
           30 March
          </span>
          2021

         <q>
          Take for example, the tentative, neutral, dispassionate, seientific description of pederasty, the love of boys that comes to the fore in the second and third volumes of L'Histoire de La sexualite. Now in part, Foucault needs to deal with what amounts to one of the great taboos of the contemporary Western world. And at least implicitly, the movement from exteriority to interiority brings about the complete repression of the possibility of the love for boys. Yet if Foucault's model and epistemological system are correct, the so-called repression of the love of boys, the construction of a taboo is not part of a repression per se but actually a means of channeling that same structure of desire. Hence Foucault's discussion of the love of boys must proceed delicately in order not to seem to be shaking the foundations of the taboo.
         </q>
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+French+and+Francophone+Philosophy&amp;rft.atitle=Foucault%27s+Body&amp;rft.volume=6&amp;rft.issue=1%E2%80%932&amp;rft.pages=59-75&amp;rft.date=1994-03&amp;rft.aulast=Schehr&amp;rft.aufirst=Lawrence+R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.pdcnet.org%2Fjfp%2Fcontent%2Fjfp_1994_0006_0001_0059_0075&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMichel+Foucault">

      </li>
      <li id="cite_note-Lehman-193">
       <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
        <b>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Lehman_193-0">
          ^
         </a>
        </b>
       </span>
       <span class="reference-text">
        <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
        <cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFDavid_Lehman1993">
         <a href="/wiki/David_Lehman" title="David Lehman">
          David Lehman
         </a>
         (21 February 1993).
         <a class="external text" href="https://www.chicagotribune.com/news/ct-xpm-1993-02-21-9303183271-story.html" rel="nofollow">
          "Michel Foucault: Vastly Influential and Rather Dangerous, too"
         </a>
         .
         <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">
          Chicago Tribune
         </a>
         .
         <q>
          As a visiting professor at Berkeley, he frequented the gay bathhouses of San Francisco, particularly the ones specializing in "S/M." He regarded sex in the light of a Faustian pact, judging it to be "worth dying for," and he refused to alter his behavior after the AIDS epidemic had broken out. There have been strong rumors to the effect that Foucault knowingly infected others with the disease. Miller's verdict: probably not. More likely, Foucault's unabated promiscuity was suicidal rather than murderous.
         </q>
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=Michel+Foucault%3A+Vastly+Influential+and+Rather+Dangerous%2C+too&amp;rft.date=1993-02-21&amp;rft.au=David+Lehman&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.chicagotribune.com%2Fnews%2Fct-xpm-1993-02-21-9303183271-story.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMichel+Foucault">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
      <li id="cite_note-Carvalho-194">
       <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
        <b>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Carvalho_194-0">
          ^
         </a>
        </b>
       </span>
       <span class="reference-text">
        <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
        <cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFCarvalho2006">
         Carvalho, John (2006).
         <a class="external text" href="https://quod.lib.umich.edu/c/ca/7523862.0004.006/--fact-and-fiction-writing-the-difference-between-suicide?rgn=main;view=fulltext" rel="nofollow">
          "Fact and Fiction: Writing the Difference Between Suicide and Death"
         </a>
         .
         <i>
          Contemporary Aesthetics
         </i>
         .
         <b>
          4
         </b>
         .
         <q>
          Miller tells a story that leads his readers to conclude that Foucault knew he had AIDS, knew it was potentially fatal and knowingly returned to those sites where the illness was spreading ... If he was not knowingly endangering the lives of others, was he not at least endangering his own life? Was this a form a suicide, a death he chose rather than a death that chose him?
         </q>
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Contemporary+Aesthetics&amp;rft.atitle=Fact+and+Fiction%3A+Writing+the+Difference+Between+Suicide+and+Death&amp;rft.volume=4&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.aulast=Carvalho&amp;rft.aufirst=John&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fquod.lib.umich.edu%2Fc%2Fca%2F7523862.0004.006%2F--fact-and-fiction-writing-the-difference-between-suicide%3Frgn%3Dmain%3Bview%3Dfulltext&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMichel+Foucault">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
      <li id="cite_note-Woodward-195">
       <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
        <b>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Woodward_195-0">
          ^
         </a>
        </b>
       </span>
       <span class="reference-text">
        <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>
        <cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFWoodward1993">
         <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_L._Woodward" title="Kenneth L. Woodward">
          Woodward, Kenneth L.
         </a>
         (31 January 1993).
         <a class="external text" href="https://www.newsweek.com/philosophers-death-wish-192046" rel="nofollow">
          "A Philosopher's Death Wish"
         </a>
         .
         <a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">
          Newsweek
         </a>
         .
         <q>
          There followed the clandestine rounds of the gay baths. In a literally painstaking hermeneutics of S&amp;M techniques, Miller explains how Foucault's fatal flirtation with death during bouts of alternating pain and pleasure offered him the e[c]static experience that had become his Nietzschean holy grail. Miller's argument is persuasive: Foucault scripted his own death as the philosophical conclusion to a life of intellectual and moral risk. That he ignored the risk of infecting others to achieve his own ends calls for stringent re-evaluation of his entire philosophical project.
         </q>
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=A+Philosopher%27s+Death+Wish&amp;rft.date=1993-01-31&amp;rft.aulast=Woodward&amp;rft.aufirst=Kenneth+L.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.newsweek.com%2Fphilosophers-death-wish-192046&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMichel+Foucault">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>

        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Frontiers+in+Psychology&amp;rft.atitle=The+True+Self.+Critique%2C+Nature%2C+and+Method&amp;rft.volume=10&amp;rft.pages=2250&amp;rft.date=2019-10-22&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC6817459%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft.issn=1664-1078&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F31695635&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3389%2Ffpsyg.2019.02250&amp;rft.aulast=Sparby&amp;rft.aufirst=Terje&amp;rft.au=Edelh%C3%A4user%2C+Friedrich&amp;rft.au=Weger%2C+Ulrich+W.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.frontiersin.org%2Farticle%2F10.3389%2Ffpsyg.2019.02250%2Ffull&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMichel+Foucault">

        </b>
       </i>
      </td>
     </tr>
    </tbody>
   </table>

    <li>
     <a class="external text" href="https://foucault.info/" rel="nofollow">
      Foucault.info.
     </a>
     Large resource site which includes extracts from Foucault's work and a comprehensive bibliography of all of Foucault's work in French
    </li>
    <li>
     <a class="external text" href="https://michel-foucault.com/" rel="nofollow">
      Foucault News.
     </a>
     Large resource site, which includes a blog with news related to Foucault research, bibliographies and other resources
    </li>
    <li>
     <a class="external text" href="https://michel-foucault.com/bibliographies/" rel="nofollow">
      Foucault bibliographies.
     </a>
     Bibliographies and links to bibliographies of, and relating to Foucault, on the Foucault News site
    </li>
    <li>
     <a class="external text" href="https://progressivegeographies.com/" rel="nofollow">
      Progressive Geographies.
     </a>
     Stuart Elden's blog and resource site. Includes extensive resources on Foucault
    </li>
